Preface 
This study is written as a master’s thesis by Maria Preilev Hansen following the Nordic5Tech double 
master’s degree program at The Technical University of Denmark and The Norwegian University of 
Science and Technology. This study was conducted within a 5month period from January to June 2022. 
The total workload account for 30 ECTS points.  
This thesis project fulfills the MSc in Industrial Ecology from NTNU as well as an Environmental En-
gineering Degree with a specialization in Residual Resource Engineering from DTU. The project was 
primarily supervised by Anders Damgaard, Senior Researcher at DTU Environment, and co-supervised 
by Helge Brattebø, Head of NTNU Bærekraft. The study was also carried out in collaboration with the 
Danish demolition and construction company G. Tscherning. I would like to thank Thomas Willumsen, 
head of the department of demolition, and Søren Tscherning, CEO at G. Tscherning, for allowing the 
utilization of all necessary data. All help, guidance, and support have been highly appreciated. 
Following the learning objectives, this study is produced, written, and designed as an article with sup-
plementary material. Therefore, the study starts as an article, followed by the references, and lastly an 
extended and thoroughly elaborated supplementary material was conducted.

      LCA on demolition types 
_________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
 
 
Life Cycle Assessment on various demolition types for office buildings in 
Denmark 
M. Preilev Hansen1,2, H. Brattebø2, A. Damgaard1 
1 Department of Environmental Engineering, Technical University of Denmark 
2 Department of Energy and Process Engineering, The Norwegian University of Science and Technology 
_______________________________________________________________________________________________ 
Abstract 
An attributional life cycle assessment (LCA) of the management of 1 m2 of demolition waste was conducted following the ILCD Handbook and 
ISO 14044:2006 for six different demolition subcategories, namely a roof-, a façade-, an interior soft strip, an interior hard strip, a complete 
demolition, and decontamination, representing different demolition waste compositions as well as demolition- and waste management practices 
on office buildings in Denmark. Data were collected from the demolition company G. Tscherning, such as waste composition, collection, use of 
machines, demolition cost, etc. while recycling efficiency at the waste treatment plant and technological efficiencies were provided by the treatment 
facilities. The objective is to illustrate the importance of including the demolition (C1-2) and associated avoided impacts (D), through an assess-
ment of 6 different demolition subcategories, to better document the impact of a full building LCA with realistic lifetime changes of building 
elements. For most of the demolition subcategories, the demolition practice and waste treatment provide an environmental burden, despite consid-
ering the benefits of material recycling and utilization of energy. The environmental burden is related to the demolition practice, special treatment, 
landfill, and transport, respectively, while the environmental savings are primarily from the recycling of metal scraps, and to a lesser extent insu-
lation, gypsum, and aggregates. The waste-to-energy plants also result in environmental savings. The data quality assessment and sensitivity 
analysis identified a range of critical parameters, suggesting better data should be obtained for the electricity consumption during demolition and 
fractional sorting of metal scrap. The study concluded that demolition and waste treatment contribute to 13% of the environmental burden from 
buildings. The complete demolition has the largest burden on climate change while the façade demolition had the largest impact on mineral 
resource scarcity. There is no trend in the amount of waste per square area and the LCIA, which indicate that the building design, building elements, 
and thus the waste composition are crucial factors to investigate to obtain a larger environmental saving from the construction sector. 
Keywords 
Life cycle assessment, demolition waste, waste management systems, climate change, resource scarcity 
Received 17th of June 2022 
____________________________________________________________________________________________
1. Introduction 
Climate change (CC) impacts from the construction sector 
and buildings have increased focus. Today, there is, however, 
neither attention to the end-of-life (EOL) of buildings nor the 
role of the demolition practice, despite the construction indus-
try accounting for approximately 35% of the total waste pro-
duction in Denmark (Pedersen, 2022). The Danish government 
has agreed on a new legislation in 2023, involving a statutory 
maximum of 12 kg CO2-eq/m2/year to be implemented for new 
buildings with a square area above 1000 m2. The calculations 
will be based on the CC impact from the production of the 
building components and their transport to the construction 
site, the construction process as well as the energy consump-
tion and maintenance/replacement of materials within 50 years 
(U. Andersen, 2021). This calculation, therefore, neglects and 
is also not required to, incorporate the impact from the demo-
lition and waste management processes of the building’s life 
cycle (Realdania, 2021). This exclusion contributes to a dese-
lection of the holistic perspective and a continuation of the ex-
isting silo-building practice (Dyck-Madsen et al., 2021). In 
2020, a Danish study investigated the emissions from the ex-
isting building practice and found that most of the sustainabil-
ity-certified buildings were already built within the statutory 
maximum of 12 kg CO2-eq/ m2/year (Tozan et al., 2021). 
To incorporate this policy, strategists call for the use of life 
cycle assessment (LCA). The European EN 15978 has made a 
specific LCA standard method for buildings (Rodriguez and 
Simonen, 2017). This LCA standard allows the quantification 
___________________

Mineral resource scarcity; SM, Supplementary Material 
==PAGE:3==
2  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
_________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
of all relevant environmental impacts, not limited to CC, of a 
building’s entire life cycle. The life cycle of a building is de-
fined by its system boundary where the holistic perspective is 
integrated with 5 stages and 17 subprocesses (Trafik- og By-
ggestyrelsen, 2016). For additional information, see Supple-
mentary Material (SM) 1.0. A literature review with 40 se-
lected articles split into construction, refurbishment, demoli-
tion, and waste management subcategories, revealed that none 
of the existing LCAs of buildings included all stages and much 
less all subprocesses (Full literature review in SM 2.0). Most 
often it is primarily the first module, the production stage (A1-
3) stage that is best known. Junnila and Horvath (2003) and Le 
et al. (2021) also argue that the construction (A4-5) and dem-
olition (C1-2) stages are negligible. Both articles claim that the 
data availability for the C1 process as well as systematically 
determined data relevant to the demolition practice were lack-
ing for the LCA calculations. Neither of the articles included 
the benefits of recycling as the waste handling life-cycle stage 
is excluded. Arguments for the exclusion and its coherent lim-
itation were not discussed in the articles. Of the 40 papers re-
viewed in total, only two studies include a reference to why 
demolition is not applied. With the snowballing tool (searching 
for articles through references in another article) it was found 
that the same reference is utilized (Tarantini et al., 2009), 
which does not include a reference to why demolition is ex-
cluded.  
 It is found that studies that include the C1 stage exclude 
important information on the waste composition such that the 
composition of waste differs from being clearly defined to be-
ing greatly assumed. An example is Pittau et al. (2019) with 
general material fractions such as 75.6% being inert waste ma-
terials or Blengini, (2009) with up to 14 waste fractions (Con-
crete, steel bars, bricks, mortar, plaster, paint, mineral insula-
tion, wood, glass, ceramics, roof tiles, plastic (PVC), alumi-
num, copper). Apart from Liu et al. (2020), who classify the 
specific waste fractions into A (recycling and landfill), B (re-
cycled aggregates and powder), and C (landfill) categories, 
neither of the remaining 40 articles specify which waste frac-
tion is treated with the different waste handling methodologies 
analyzed. In general studies on demolition waste include clean 
and heavy waste fractions but exclude the removal of pollu-
tants such as asbestos contained materials and other hazardous 
substances (Kim et al., 2016). Concerning the LCAs of build-
ings that incorporated the refurbishment (B4-5) stage, these 
LCAs primarily focus on the analysis of new efficient build-
ings thus neglecting the existing building stock or the complete 
demolition before the construction of larger new buildings. In 
addition to this, the studies dealing with the B4-5 stage of 
buildings only include environmental gains compared to the 
existing building and therefore not with the new equivalent 
construction (Marique and Rossi, 2018). Furthermore, it is dif-
ficult to determine the operation stage from the design stage as 
well as the service lifetime and the proportions of the individ-
ual building components that need to be replaced (Le et al., 
2021). The service life of a building determines the number of 
replacements of construction products and the total operational 
energy use (Vilches et al., 2017) so that parts with shorter life-
times have the production (A1-3) and waste management (C2-
3) included twice in the calculations. This is an inaccurate pro-
cedure as this study has revealed that a demolition (process not 
included in the calculations) only rarely includes the removal 
of a single element. 
Overall, this illustrates that the demolition and waste man-
agement process is significantly neglected in the study of LCA 
for buildings and can illustrate larger impacts for studies that 
include updated data for the case study in question. Today, the 
methodology for the inclusion of the C1 activity stage varies 
significantly. Le et al. (2021) specify that the primary source 
of energy to be used at the C1 stage was diesel from heavy 
machines like loaders, trucks, and excavators. On the contrary, 
Junnila and Horvath (2003) do not specify which processes 
have been included or where the data has been collected in the 
LCA for both the A5 and the C1 stages. Limited studies eval-
uate the diesel burned and energy needed for A5, B4-5, and C1 
of the buildings (Ghose et al., 2019; Itard and Klunder, 2007; 
Pomponi et al., 2015; Rabani et al., 2021; Weiler et al., 2017). 
As found in the literature review, the best results are obtained 
through the inclusion of collected data from the demolition ac-
tivity, e.g., energy consumption, fuel for transportation, and 
machinery as well as additional resources, which can vary for 
each respective case study. Typically, the transportation dis-
tances are contextualized and normalized to the country or pro-
ject in focus, which makes comparisons more difficult (Ne-
matchoua et al., 2019).  
The objective of this study is to illustrate the importance of 
including the demolition (C1-2), waste management (C3-4), 
and associated avoided impacts (D), through an assessment of 
6 different demolition subcategories. The calculations are 
based on waste composition and energy inputs (e.g., fuel from 
machinery, waste transportation, and energy consumption). 
The results will be the potential impacts, not limited to CC, 
from the EOL process of the buildings. To better comprehend 
Fig 1 General system description and system boundaries of the LCA model system for the demolition subcategories.  
==PAGE:4==
3  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
_________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
their potential impacts from demolition and maintenance, the 
life cycle impact assessment (LCIA) results will be compared 
to other building LCAs, to better document the impact of a full 
building LCA with realistic lifetime changes of elements. This 
study shall provide the basis for the influence of the combined 
demolition and waste treatment process on the LCA of build-
ings.  
2. Methodology 
This study adheres to the requirements of the ILCD Hand-
book (European Commission, 2010a) and the ISO 14044 (ISO, 
2006). Six different demolition categories were included in the 
study, to cover the different aspects of different demolition 
practices as well as to represent their variations in waste com-
position, demolition costs, levels of recycling, and waste treat-
ment systems (Detailed goal and scope in the SM 3.0). The 
demolition activities include:  
• 
3 roof demolitions, which is the removal of a roof. 
• 
3 combined roof and façade demolition as well as an ad-
ditional façade demolition only including concrete re-
moval.  
• 
6 interior soft strip demolitions, where a soft strip demoli-
tion is the partial removal of the interior of a building. 
• 
5 interior hard strip demolitions, which is the demolition 
of a building where only the structural frame and compo-
nents of the building remain. 
• 
3 complete demolitions, which is the complete removal of 
all building materials, including foundations.  
• 
5 decontamination projects, which is the removal of haz-
ardous substances in building materials. 
 
2.1 The LCA approach 
The LCA case study is a real-size systematic comparative 
assessment used to highlight the hotspots related to the B4-5 
and C1 stages and the main difference in the effect on office 
buildings in Denmark. By neglecting the large-scale conse-
quences of a change in demolition- and waste treatment prac-
tice in the background system the decision context falls within 
situation A. Following the EN 15978, this study is based on the 
attributional cut-off modeling approach (Rodriguez and Simo-
nen, 2017). Marginal processes and average data were used for 
the modeling of the background system (e.g., marginal Danish 
average mix based on natural gas for electricity and natural gas 
for heating), as recommended by the ILCD Handbook (Euro-
pean Commission, 2010a). 
Figure 1 illustrates the general system boundaries for the 
various demolition subcategories. The model systems will be 
more specific for each demolition project and demolition sub 
category. Here the LCIA in this study is solely based on the  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
C1-C4 stages of the EN 15978 and thus the waste is introduced 
to the system as deconstruction.  
The functional unit (FU) is the management of 1 m2 of 
demolition waste for six different demolition subcategories in 
Denmark. It is also chosen to investigate the environmental im-
pacts with a FU of managing demolition waste per largest 
waste fraction, managing demolition waste per working day, 
managing demolition waste per demolition cost, and managing 
demolition waste per person-hours of work for the project. 
With these units, it will be possible to identify the best FU to 
use for further analysis.  
No impurities have been considered in the sorted fractions. 
The waste treatment processes included in the study are con-
trolled landfills, material recycling for dry waste materials, 
special treatment of hazardous waste, and Waste-to-Energy 
(WtE) for the combustible fractions. The residues from the in-
cineration plant include incineration ash to landfill while the 
residues from the material recycling facility are based on the 
efficiencies stated by RGS Nordic, Norrecco, and Dansk 
Miljøforbedring. The demolition process includes the con-
sumption of electricity and water, fuel for machinery, commut-
ing of employees, and delivery of equipment. The construction 
process and the upstream processes of material production are 
excluded from the assessment. Material recovery is included 
through system expansion with different substitution factors 
depending on the recycling technology and quality of the sec-
ondary product, for each waste fraction to substitute the virgin 
materials. Capital goods have not been included in this model. 
The geographical scope is Zealand, Denmark. Data collec-
tion was done from the Danish demolition company G. 
Tscherning (site location, waste composition, demolition ac-
tivities such as energy consumption, internal transportation, 
working hours, the total number of truckloads from the demo-
lition site, and weight-based truckloads from the first material 
treatment facility to the final EOL treatment). Danish waste 
treatment facilities have provided recycling efficiencies of 
clean waste fractions while the waste-to-energy (WtE) incin-
eration plants provided specific energy efficiencies. Inputs and 
outputs from the waste treatment facilities (the recycling and 
substitution percentages) are defined as parameters with a nor-
mal and uniform distribution where the sensitivity values are 
the maximum and minimum values for the uniform distribution 
and the average value and the standard deviation for the normal 
distribution. The parameters can be found in Table 3.  
The 26 different demolition projects spanned from 2018 to 
2022. Lastly, the total accounting for each of the individual 
projects has been provided to form a cost estimate in each dem-
olition subcategory. The software used is EASETECH V3.4.1 
(Clavreul et al., 2014). The transport processes and the treat-
ment processes for recycling as well as landfilling, special

treatment of hazardous waste, reprocessing, and substitution of 
materials have been collected from the ecoinvent database ver-
sion 2.7.3. Due to a lack of inventory data from Denmark, 
Switzerland was used as a proxy as it is assumed that the tech-
nological processes are similar to the best practice from Den-
mark. For each process and parameter either a uniform or nor-
malized distribution has been chosen to incorporate all uncer-
tainties. All impact categories are found with the same LCIA 
methodology: ReCiPe MidPoint 2016 method with the hierar-
chic perspective. See the impact categories in Table 1. Long-
term effects are excluded from the study as well as the impact 
categories water use and land use as these impact categories 
are largely dependent on the geographical location contrib-
uting to great uncertainties. Fossil resource depletion has also 
been excluded as there cannot be obtained normalized results 
for this impact category. CC and mineral resource scarcity 
(RD-m) are the impact categories that will be in focus during 
the interpretation of the LCIA results. 
 
2.2 The life cycle inventory 
The following section provides a summary of the important 
technical specifications of the modeling in EASETECH. The 
detailed version of the life cycle inventory (LCI) can be found 
in SM 4.0. Here the model processes are introduced with the 
same procedure for each project in subsections of Roof demo-
lition, Façade demolition, Interior soft strip demolition, Inte-
rior hard strip demolition, Complete demolition, and Decon-
tamination. The waste composition of the projects in each dem-
olition subcategory was split into waste fractions of asbestos, 
asphalt, glass, concrete, gypsum, hazardous waste, mixed 
heavy fractions (including brick, roof bricks, asphalt, and other 
heavier fractions), mixed waste split into category 1 and 2 (in-
cluded metal, gypsum, combustibles, heavy fractions, and in-
sulation for category 2), other combustibles, plaster waste from 
the sandblasting of concrete walls, insulation, shredder, slag, 
soil, and wood). Here the hazardous waste is always sent to 
special treatment, while asbestos, plaster waste, and other 
waste with contaminated substances above the limit value for 
clean waste are sent to landfills. It is project specific if com-
bustibles are sent straight for incineration or to the recycling 
facility to be cut into fitted pieces before entering the incinera-
tion opening. The information about each project is obtained 
from G. Tscherning. The fractional waste composition from 
each of the demolition projects is visualized in Figure 2. Each 
waste fraction had its own chemical composition which was 
inserted and used in the EASETECH modeling.  
All projects have been given subcategory identification let-
ters as well as project specific numbers to tell them apart in the 
individual result analysis. “R” for roof, “RF” for combined 
roof and façade, “F” for façade, “SS” for soft stripping, “HS” 
for hard stripping, “CD” for complete demolition, and “DC” 
for decontamination. An example is the complete demolition 
project CD-2. 
 
2.2.1 Project explanation of CD-2 
For CD-2 the project includes decontamination, demoli-
tion, and disposal of 4 buildings in Copenhagen. The total area 
of the buildings, including their basements, is 3930 m2. A 
building connected to one of the demolished buildings must be 
fully functional during the entire demolition period, which cre-
ated the need for a separating wall. In this way, the remaining 
building and part of the basement could be cut off from the 
demolition. This presented additional obstacles in the form of 
care in the demolition practice. This was considered early in 
the design phase of the demolition practice so that the cost of 
securing this wall from damage during demolition was in-
cluded. In general, the demolition contractor had to cut through 
the existing basement, both in the base plate, walls, and "ceil-
ing/roof" with a diamond cutter. The diamond cutting has not 
been modeled, as it was not possible to find a similar process 
in the ecoinvent database. This is assumed to be of minor im-
portance as the impact primarily would be from carbon emis-
sions in the use phase and is thus expected to have less im-
portance by the repeated use of the tools (Li et al., 2017).  
CD-2 also included sandblasting of 51844 m2, and suction 
and wash down of 3.4 t waste with 3 m3 water by subcontrac-
tors. After consulting the company, KM Afrensning, it is 
known that sandblasting utilizes 0.34 l/m2 (KM Afrensning, 
2021). The suction and wash-down are modeled as water and 
the practice consumes 4.7 l diesel/hour (Norva24 and Søren-
sen, 2021). In addition to this, the concrete and other heavy 
fractions, in total 3123 t were crushed on site and used as ag-
gregates and soil stabilization. This process requires 0.5 l die-
sel/ton crushed heavy fraction material (MIS Recycling, 2020). 
Given that 60% of 76% heavy fractions remained on site, the 
transportation vehicle is set to a 7.5-16-ton EURO5 lorry truck 
from the site to the recycling facility. The project started on the 
4th of June 2019 and lasted until the 21st of January 2020 which

Waste transport was done between the demolition site and

This information has been provided for all 26 projects as

 2.3 Sensitivity and uncertainty analysis 
To investigate sensitive inputs on the results, a sensitivity 
analysis has been conducted for each of the parameters. This 
is done by finding the sensitivity ratio with a perturbation 
analysis, as shown in Equation (1). Following this, the sensi-
tivity coefficient has been calculated to obtain results on the 
most sensitive parameter to each impact category, see Equa-
tion (2). Here i = 1, …, n tested parameters in each model 
while j = 1, …, m is the impact categories in the characteriza-
tion method selected for the calculation of the LCIA (18 for 
the ReCiPe method). The sensitivity results were normalized 
to get more accurate average results for each demolition sub-
category. Lastly, an uncertainty analysis has been made to de-
termine the most uncertain parameters and with this, find the 
key inputs and parameters to the model.  
𝑆𝑅𝑖
𝑗=
( Δ𝑅𝑒𝑠𝑢𝑙𝑡
𝑅𝑒𝑠𝑢𝑙𝑡𝑖𝑛𝑖𝑡)
𝑗
( Δ𝑃𝑎𝑟𝑎𝑚𝑒𝑡𝑒𝑟
𝑃𝑎𝑟𝑎𝑚𝑒𝑡𝑒𝑟𝑖𝑛𝑖𝑡)
𝑖
 
(1) 
𝑆𝐶𝑖
𝑗=
(Δ𝑅𝑒𝑠𝑢𝑙𝑡)𝑗
(Δ𝑃𝑎𝑟𝑎𝑚𝑒𝑡𝑒𝑟)𝑖
 
(2) 
An uncertainty analysis was conducted with the Pedigree 
matrix (Weidema et al., 2013) where the parameters, chosen 
to model processes, and input data are weighted and scored 
according to how well they are deemed true to the reality they 
represent. Here, the inventory data quality is judged based on 
its reliability (R), completeness (C), and representativeness 
both geographical correlation (Gc), temporal correlation (Tc) 
as well as Further technical correlation (Ftc). It was possible 
to calculate the data quality rating (DQR), which is calculated 
with the equation below. The DQR value can be set between 
the minimum (1) and maximum (5) value score.  
𝐷𝑄𝑅= ∑𝑑𝑎𝑡𝑎 𝑞𝑢𝑎𝑙𝑖𝑡𝑦 𝑖𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑐𝑎𝑡𝑜𝑟
 
+ 𝑤𝑒𝑎𝑘𝑒𝑠𝑡 𝑖𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑐𝑎𝑡𝑜𝑟· 4
𝑛𝑢𝑚𝑏𝑒𝑟 𝑜𝑓 𝑑𝑎𝑡𝑎 𝑞𝑢𝑎𝑙𝑖𝑡𝑦 𝑖𝑛𝑑𝑖𝑐𝑎𝑡𝑜𝑟𝑠+ 4
 
(3) 
The sensitivity analysis was compared to the results re-
vealed in the data quality assessment to obtain the most criti-
cal parameters (Weidema et al., 2013). Here the sensitive pa-
rameters are divided into a low, medium, and high sensitivity 
subcategory, where the low category is found for a normal-
ized sensitivity ratio (NSR) below 0.-3, the high is found for 
an NSR above 0.7, while the moderate is for the NSR in be-
tween. The DQR has also been categorized in subcategories 
where a DQR<1.6: low, 1.6<DQR>3: moderate, and DQR>3: 
high. 
 
2.4 Cost estimate 
The cost estimate has been calculated for each demolition 
project and split into three sectoral categories: Demolition, 
Waste treatment, and Transport to analyze which element of 
demolition is the costliest. For the demolition activity, the 
cost includes labor, commuting, machines, subcontractors, 
and additional equipment. The waste treatment category was 
split into recycling, incineration, landfill, and special treat-
ment. The cost was the disposal of each element from the 
==PAGE:8==
7  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
_________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
demolition perspective. For the transport activity, it was not 
possible to split the cost from delivery of equipment and 
transportation of waste fractions. The cost estimate is only a 
reference of the cost from the demolition perspective, and 
thus the cost from the individual waste treatment plants, or 
external transportation and subcontractor companies have 
been excluded. The carbon emissions costs and income from 
each demolition project as well as the sale of recycled mate-
rials have also been excluded from this study.  
3. Results and discussion 
This section will present the characterized and normalized 
LCIA results on CC and RD-m for each of the demolition 
subcategories. Furthermore, a contribution analysis has been 
made for each of the demolition projects in their respective 
demolition subcategory. Here, the impacts have been aggre-
gated into 7 life cycle stages: Demolition (C1), transport from 
a demolition site to waste treatment facility (C2), Recycling 
(C3), Incineration (C3), special treatment incineration (C3), 
Landfill (C4), and material reprocessing (D) (Full result anal-
ysis in SM 5.0). The interpretation will also include an under-
standing of the robustness of the results, and limitations, as 
well as a further outlook. 
 
3.1 Comparison of the demolition subcategories 
The average environmental impact in the six different 
demolition subcategories is visualized in Figure 3 with char-
acterized values for CC and Figure 4 for RD-m.  
From Figure 3 it is illustrated that the average value for 
the complete demolition has the largest impact on CC, which 
links to this demolition subcategory also having the largest 
amount of heavy waste per total area. From G. Tscherning it 
is found that complete demolition is only 19 cases of 236 in a 
year yet still accounting for 64%, 32%, 21%, and 38% of the 
total waste sent to the recycling, incineration, landfill, and 
special treatment, respectively (data from the accounting year 
2020/2021). No correlation between the total waste amount 
per square meters and CC impact was found. With an R2 value 
of 0.42, it is calculated that for every additional ton of 
waste/m2 the impact increases by 27 kg CO2-eq/m2. 
The largest environmental saving is found for the hard- 
and soft-strip demolition subcategories, respectively. These 
are the demolition subcategories that produce the least heavy 
waste fractions per demolished area. The variation for the 
hard strip demolition makes this demolition impact more un-
certain. In the worst-case scenario, a hard strip demolition can 
be more harmful to the CC impact category than decontami-
nation or a roof demolition. For the construction year, the 
only trend found for the environmental impacts was for pro-
jects built between 1950 and 1970. Here the waste contributed 
to an impact burden, related to more polluted building ele-
ments (especially asbestos) not appropriate for recycling. 
From the contribution analysis, it was revealed that the 
demolition share of total impact contributes to the greatest 
burden for CC, while for RD-m the largest contribution and 
saving is found for the material reprocessing and substitution. 
This trend is the same across all demolition subcategories 
(See SM Excel). In most cases, except for decontamination, 
the demolition activity contributes to approximately 40% of 
the entire environmental impact on CC, which proves that 
demolition plays a vital role relative to the impact on CC for 
a waste management system. As the demolition practice, it-
self was the most significant contributor to CC, it was chosen

on Danish office buildings. The green bar illustrate the demolition share of the total impact 
Fig 4 The total environmental impact on mineral resource depletion in characterized values [kgCu-eq/m2] for the average of the six demolition 
subcategory actives on Danish office building. The green bar illustrate the demolition share of the total impact.  
==PAGE:9==
8  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
_________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
to investigate the process contribution from demolition. 
Within the demolition practice, the largest contribution is the 
consumption of electricity for the demolition site accommo-
dation. This impact varied from 28.8% for a complete demo-
lition, which also included both an excavator and subcontrac-
tor use of a wheel loader to 99.8% for a decontamination pro-
ject with no use of larger machines (See SM 5.0 for an ex-
tended analysis of the demolition process). The electricity 
consumption for the demolition site accommodation is calcu-
lated with the parameter Demo_elec which depends on the 
demolished area. Here the complete demolition has the most 
waste per area and therefore, a lower contribution from the 
electricity consumption. This affects the overall process con-
tribution from demolition on the overall CC burden to be 
smaller, despite more machines being used for the complete 
demolition projects. This is also partly related to more waste 
being utilized as product substitution compared to decontam-
ination with no material substitution and where the demoli-
tion process contributes to 90% of the total environmental 
burden.  
The waste incineration also contributes to environmental 
savings for both the CC and the RD-m impact category. Haz-
ardous waste treatment, landfill, recycling, and transportation 
are all processes that contribute slightly to the environmental 
burden in both impact categories, except for decontamination 
where the hazardous waste treatment and transport contribute 
to a larger burden. It could be argued that the decontamination 
project analyzed in this study is not a demolition activity but 
rather the sole removal of hazardous substances. Today, how-
ever, almost all demolition projects include decontamination 
to some extent. The decontamination activity analyzed in this 
study instead provides a supplement to the environmental im-
pact of the existing demolition practice and inside knowledge 
on the effect of the future impacts if the removal of hazardous 
substances should increase or decrease.  
For the RD-m, the visualized results in Figure 4 illustrate 
that all demolished waste management systems, except de-
contamination, contribute to impact saving in the RD-m. The 
largest variation within the demolition subcategories can be 
found for those with the greatest environmental savings. In 
general, the demolition projects, with a larger part of the 
waste composition consisting of contaminated substances, 
had little tendency to resource exploitation while projects 
with a larger amount of metal scrap contributed to larger re-
source exploitation. Heavy fractions are also exploited on a 
larger scale, but the resulting impacts are not significant, 
which could be related to copper being a globally scarce ma-
terial while aggregates are a locally scarce material for Den-
mark. A local characterization factor for the mineral resource 
impact category would provide more accurate results for this 
impact category from a Danish perspective. 
With a unit of Cu-eq, it is expected that more copper is 
retrieved from the metal scrap fraction compared to any other 
construction material reprocessed from a demolition site. The 
endpoint characterization factor can be expressed as other 
surplus ore potentials (SOP). According to Vieira et al. 
(2017), sufficient data is found for 18 mineral resources in-
cluding gold, silver, zinc, iron, lead, tin, nickel, cobalt, chro-
mium, and aluminum which primarily represent the metal 
scrap fraction. Other mineral resources include antimony, 
lithium, manganese, molybdenum, niobium, phosphorus, and 
uranium. Neither of these mineral resources represents aggre-
gates, gypsum, or insulation which could significantly impact 
the way the RD-m impact category is interpreted according to 
demolition waste. It could be suggested that minerals without 
sufficient data for the SOP could be derived from a financial 
aspect e.g., the price rather than the empirical cumulative 
grade-tonnage relationship.  
No trend can be found for the percentage that demolition 
impact RD-m. The burden from demolition can again, be at-
tributed to the electricity consumption, however, the transport 
of equipment also contributes significantly. This illustrates 
that the saving from RD-m could be additionally increased by 
limiting the transport of waste and equipment with better and 
faster project planning as well as limiting the period for which 
the site accommodation is used.  
 
3.2 Roof demolition 
For the roof demolition, the project with the removal of 
an aluminum roof contributed to the lowest environmental ef-
fect in the CC and the RD-m impact categories. This was re-
lated to the substitution ratio set for the waste fraction of 
metal scrap as well as the well-developed recycling process 
for metal. Within the steel substitution process, the avoidance 
of burning fossil fuels and methane for the virgin production 
of steel is what contributes to the largest emission reduction. 
For every kg of metal scrap that is being reprocessed the im-
pact is 0.12 kg CO2-eq which is a minimal impact compared 
to the production of new steel materials (2.82 kg CO2-eq).  
The removal of the slate eternity roof contributes to the 
largest emission of CO2-eq as there is no material recycling. 
Had the production of the materials been within the system 
boundary it is expected that the impact burden on RD-m 
would be larger. Despite being very different in project spec-
ifications, the roof demolition subcategory had the smallest 
variation compared to the other demolition subcategories for 
the emissions of kg CO2-eq/m2 per project.  
 
3.3 Facade demolition 
For the façade demolition projects, which include primar-
ily heavy fractions e.g., a brick wall, the burden on the CC 
impact category was the largest. For the façade demolition 
with a single waste fraction; concrete, the environmental im-
pact is much larger, which is attributed to no incineration pro-
cess contributing to an environmental saving. Additionally, 
the recycling process contributes to a larger part of the CC 
impact for projects with a larger share of heavy fractions, as 
it is assumed that the recycling facilities utilize more fuel with 
the crushing of concrete and other heavy fractions. Further-
more, the heavier fractions are less valuable material substi-
tutes for the RD-m impact category.  
 
3.4 Soft strip demolition 
The soft strip demolition projects with the smallest waste 
amount contribute to the largest burden on the CC impact cat-
egory, while the largest saving can be found in the projects 
with a greater fraction of shredder waste in the overall waste 
composition.  
The contribution analysis for the soft strip demolition pro-
jects revealed that in the CC impact category, it is either the 
demolition or incineration process which contributes the most 
across the various projects. The contribution from the waste-
to-energy plant as well as the substance specific emissions 
from the waste incineration process itself was analyzed. For 
the waste-to-energy-plant process, the electricity and heat 
substitution contribute to the largest saving, respectively. The 
electricity mix from waste incineration is better than the elec-
tricity mix from the current Danish grid which is primarily 
based on natural gas. The same applies to heat substitution. 
==PAGE:10==
9  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
_________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
From the substance specific contributions in the WtE, the 
burning of carbon contained products such as wood and other 
combustibles, contribute to the largest burden for the CC im-
pact category. The removal of uranium, iron, and nickel from 
the incineration cleaning process contributes to the environ-
mental saving in the RD-m impact category. The saving is 
related to the avoided use of nuclear power plants in Sweden, 
and thus this saving contributes to uncertainty for the RD-m 
impact category. The removal of iron and nickel could be re-
lated to bad sorting and mixed waste categories. Nickel is in-
cluded in construction waste on painted surfaces.  
 
3.5 Hard strip demolition 
The projects with the largest total waste amount, largest 
variation in waste composition, and which also involved the 
removal of asbestos and hazardous waste had the greatest 
emissions of CO2-eq. The hard strip demolition project with 
the lowest emissions of CO2-eq had the smallest demolished 
area. Since similar fractions are sent to a waste handler from 
a hard strip demolition, the effect on CC is limited by the 
number of different waste fractions and treatment methods. 
The difference in the composition included waste fractions 
such as asbestos, hazardous waste, mixed waste (category 1), 
soil, and wood. No average value on RD-m can be found for 
the impact per demolished area. Within the material repro-
cessing and substitution, the saving on the CC and the RD-m 
impact category from asphalt, concrete, and wood is exceed-
ingly small, given that the amount being recycled directly or 
substituted is relatively small, see Table 3. Lastly, with more 
waste, the impact from the demolition activity on the mineral 
resource category is larger. 
 
3.6 Complete demolition 
This is the demolition subcategory with the greatest vari-
ation in the results of the demolition subcategory. This type 
of demolition also provides the most amount of waste which 
contributes to the larger margin for the impacts depending on 
the waste composition, and how the waste is separated into 
fractions and handled at the waste facilities. Furthermore, the 
complete demolition is the demolition activity with the larg-
est effect on CC. The lowest CC impact is found for the pro-
ject where heavy fractions were crushed on the demolition 
site contributing to a saving in transport and fuel consumption 
at the recycling facility.  
For the complete demolitions, there is a larger variation in 
the contribution from the various activities. Demolition still 
contributes to the largest burden in both the CC and the RD-
m impact category; however, special treatment and recycling 
have a larger contribution, compared to the previously ana-
lyzed demolition subcategories. Additionally, the excavator 
is used significantly more for complete demolitions, related 
to the removal of fundaments and heavier fractions. For the 
project where the heavy fractions are crushed on site, there is 
less transport of materials within the demolition practice.  
No significant average value for the RD-m can be found 
across the impact per demolished area. Within the material 
reprocessing category, the reprocessing and substitution of 
shredder, insulation, and the crushing and substitution of 
gravel have the largest contributions to a saving in the CC 
impact category. Compost contributes to a negative impact on 
CC but has almost no impact on the RD-m impact category. 
 
3.7 Decontamination 
Despite the decontamination projects being the smallest 
form of demolition, it has the second largest CC impact. This 
is the only demolition subcategory where a burden was cal-
culated for the RD-m impact category as this is a demolition 
practice that includes the removal of non-recyclable hazard-
ous substances.  
For the decontamination projects, the demolition process 
contributes to the largest burden in the CC impact category, 
except for one project where waste is only sent for special 
treatment. Within the special treatment process, the burning 
of fossil carbon dioxide contributes to the largest burden for 
the CC impact category. For the RD-m, the complete burning 
of iron, nickel, and copper contribute to an environmental 
burden. The elevated temperatures for a hazardous waste 
treatment facility convert usable to non-usable substances, 
and combust all substances bound to material with the haz-
ardous substances. For two decontamination projects, it was 
not possible to obtain results on the floor area of the buildings.

3.8 Combination of demolition types 
The complete demolition in essence is a combination of 
the combined roof and façade, hard strip demolition, and de-
contamination with additional building structure and founda-
tion. Therefore, the average impacts from these demolition 
subcategories were added to test if the individual refurbish-
ment of a building contributed is a better alternative com-
pared to complete demolition. The calculations revealed that 
the complete demolition (44.8 kg CO2-eq/m2) had a much 
larger burden effect on CC than the combined demolition of 
roof, façade, hard strip, and decontamination (6.8 kg CO2-
eq/m2). It is, thus, favorable to refurbish a building up to 6 
times before a complete demolition is the better alternative. 
The standard deviation (StD = 13.2 kg CO2-eq/m2) from the 
combined demolition calculation did however illustrate great 
uncertainties. A worst-case scenario, with the largest possible 
impact from the combined demolition (19.9 kg CO2-eq/m2) 
still illustrated refurbishment to be more beneficial compared 
to the complete demolition. This calculation does not include 
the effects from the production of new façade, roof, or interior 
building elements, and therefore the overall impact from a re-
furbishment will be larger. For the mineral resource, it is also 
calculated that a combined demolition, (-0.67 kg Cu-eq/m2), 
is better than the complete demolition (-0.09 kg Cu-eq/m2) 
with a greater amount of less valuable recycling and substitu-
tion rates for the heavy fractions.  
 
3.9 Normalized LCIA results 
The normalized impact has also been found for each en-
vironmental impact category. Here the impact category of hu-
man toxicity (cancer) (HT-c) is significantly more impacted 
compared to the remaining categories. This is due to the land-
filling of incineration ash which is estimated to contain a large 
amount of chromium. Waste from demolition only rarely con-
tains chromium above the limit value set by the Danish mu-
nicipalities individually. Chromium is most often found in 
paint and plastic coating on separate building elements so that 
the concentration of chromium in the entire waste composi-
tion becomes extremely small. The screening of hazardous 
substances was analyzed for each of the 26 analyzed demoli-
tion projects. This revealed that the limit value for chromium 
was exceeded between 230-800% (limit value 1000 mg/kg 
according to Danish standards) in individual building ele-
ments for 5 projects. The impact of human toxicity (cancer)is 
therefore very uncertain. 
Table 4 illustrates the environmental impact of the six 
demolition subcategories. In general, the complete demoli-
tion has the greatest impact on each of the impact categories, 
except RD-m and ozone depletion (ODP) for which decon-
tamination has the greater impact. Apart from HT-c, ODP, 
photo- 
chemical oxidant formation, both human health (POF-hh) and 
ecosystem quality (POF-eq) are affected to most. It is inter-
esting to observe that CC is one of the categories which is less 
influenced by the demolition and waste treatment activities 
compared to the other impact categories. Despite this obser-
vation, CC and RD-M are the impact categories which is most 
widely discussed across LCA studies as revealed in the liter-
ature review. For each of the demolition subcategories, the 
CC impact varies from being a factor of 100 (for the com-
bined roof and façade- soft strip-, and hard strip demolition), 
a factor of 1000 (roof demolition), and to a factor of 10000 
(for the complete demolition and decontamination projects) 
larger than for the RD-m impact category. This reveals that 
demolition has a larger impact on CC compared to RD-m. By 
including the production of building elements, currently out-
side the scope definition, this differential factor could be de-
creased. Furthermore, a change in the normalization factor to 
include local conditions rather than global, could increase or 
decrease the impact from the reprocessing and substitution of 
different building materials, e.g., for concrete or aggregates 
for which Denmark is running out of area to practice mineral 
exploitation (Smith and Andersen, 2020). 
The contribution analysis for the POF-hh, POF-eq, and 
the ODP impact category is analyzed in-depth here as these 
were the categories that had the largest impacts. The demoli-
tion practice also contributes to the largest impacts on both 
POF-eq and POF-hh. Within the demolition process, the sig-
nificant impact is related to the burning of nitrogen oxides 
from employee commuting. Transportation alone is the pro-
cess with the largest impact on ODP, due to emissions of 
phosphate on the ground, ammonium ion in the surface water, 
and dinitrogen monoxide.

in the FU for the CC and RD-m impact category. Here the 
functional unit varies from impact per square meter to impact 
per day, impact per person-hour of work, impact per cost (in 
1000 DKK), and impact per largest waste fraction in kg. The 
results can be found in Table 5 (see SM excel for the remain-
ing impact categories).  
For the CC impact category, the impact per day contrib-
utes to a larger impact as well as a larger variation in the im-
pact compared to the remaining FU. Especially for the soft 
strip demolition results in both impact categories. This is re-
lated to one project which has a large saving impact related to 
the reprocessing of steel, and which took only four days to

execute. The resulting impact was great enough so that the 
impact from the other projects in the same demolition subcat-

The FU which creates the least variation between the pro-

waste fraction. Here the decontamination is the main contrib-

to each other as well as individually. This is because these FU 
are temporally, spatially, and project specific. The FU per 
area can be obtained more easily compared to the largest 
waste fraction as this FU will be known first when the project 
is demolished. Both FU has good arguments and can be used 
widely. 
For the RD-m, the best FU is found per area with the 
smallest variation in the results. The same argument as for CC 
goes for the FU per day, per person-hours of work, and cost. 
The FU per largest waste fraction is unusable as the results 
become too small and the comparability less significant. 
  
3.11 Data quality assessment 
Table 6 illustrates a summary of the DQR and data quality 
level (DQL) for each model process. All DQRs and DQLs for 
each parameter and process can be found in SM Excel. The 
outcome of the data quality assessment is:  
• 
The recycling and substitution of metal as well as the 
transportation process activity are the least uncertain. As 
these processes and parameters are based on well docu-
mented and updated model processes from a close geo-
graphical area. The largest uncertainty is the reliability of 
recycled steel amount from the shredder and mixed waste 
fractions.  
• 
The demolition process activities together with the recy-
cling, and incineration process activities are reliable as 
the data has been provided directly from the supplier and 
recipient. The most uncertain factor is the model pro-
cesses which are dated back to 2008 and because the 
modeling of machines has been done on a general level.  
• 
The recycling and substitution of insulation, gypsum, 
wood, asphalt, and composting are the most uncertain 
process activities in the reprocessing of materials cate-
gory. This is related to inadequate scores for the Tc, Gc, 
and Ftc as well as their reliability in the recycling of used 
material from the demolition site rather than unused but 
wasted material from the construction site. 
• 
Landfilling and special treatment are the process activi-
ties with the most uncertainty and have been deemed of 
low quality. This is related to the model processes being 
from 1994, the location being from Switzerland, and the 
further technological advancement presumed updated 
since 1994 which contribute to poor Tc, Gc, and Ftc. Fur-
thermore, the R and C score has been deemed uncertain 
as the impacts from e.g., incineration ash is process spe-
cific emissions and not linked to the demolition waste.

the demolition site (Demo_elec), the fraction of combustibles 
which is sent to incineration (Inc_combustible), as well as the

EURO4 or EURO6 did not impact the LCIA results signifi-
cantly for either of the demolition subcategories. This is re-
lated to the transportation process contributing insignificantly 
to the LCIA results for the individual demolition projects. No 
parameter was given for the transportation as the input to the 
model was given in exact kilometers changing for each pro-
ject location and waste receiver 
 
3.13 Critical data 
In this section, the most important parameters are identified 
by mapping the NSR values against their respective DQR val-
ues as visualized in Figure 5 for the CC impact category. 
Each parameter has been given a number as defined in Table 
3. If a parameter is uncertain, it is likely to be subject to 
change which will influence the overall result according to 
how sensitive the parameter is. Here the parameter Demo_ 
elec (3) is the most critical parameter for the decontamination. 
This parameter is also of high importance for the combined 
roof and façade, the complete, the hard strip, and the roof 
demolition, respectively. Better results are required to be ob-
tained for the electricity consumption (e.g., data collection or

testing specifically for a demolition site) to limit the uncer-

Other critical parameters include RGS_diesel (18) for the 
complete demolition, as the fuel consumption at the recycling 
facility also includes fuel from the wastewater treatment fa-
cility sharing the location of the primary waste handler RGS. 
More precise data could be provided for the fuel consumption 
needed for each fraction instead of an average per total 
amount of waste treated at the recycling facility. Re_metal 
(15) for the soft strip, as well as façade and complete demoli-
tion, is placed in the moderate box in the middle, and thus 
better overall results on the demolition and waste manage-
ment impact could be more limited if the waste fraction shred-
der were divided into clean fractions of steel, iron, aluminum, 
copper (from wires), etc. Then the substitution and recycling 
percentage would also increase with more certainty. The re-
maining parameters either have better data quality which will 
be less frequent to change or smaller sensitivity to be identi-
fied as important parameters.  
The most critical parameters in the mineral resource im-
pact category are also the Demo_elec for the decontamination 
project and the complete demolition followed by Re_metal 
from the hard strip, roof, and soft strip demolition, respec-
tively. As Demo_elec is a parameter of high importance in 
both impact categories, it would have been interesting to see 
how a change in the marginal energy mix had impacted the 
results. A change in the electricity mix from natural gas to 
wind power would decrease the current environmental sav-
ing, as electricity from wind is generated with less pollution 
than the combustion of waste. Such a hypothetical system 
could change the demolition practice into including more ma-
terial recycling rather than energy recovery. 
 
3.14 Cost estimates 
The cost of the demolition practice has been split into 
three categories: the demolition activities, the various mate-
rial treatment options, and the transportation. For all demoli-
tion subcategories, the demolition activity alone is the costli-
est. The largest cost within the demolition activity is found 
for labor, which is also the least environmentally harmful 
“process” compared to electricity consumption, transport, and 
machines. As labor is the costliest activity it enhances the 
trend of conducting a demolition as quickly as possible with-
out sensitive care to the building element that is to be decon-
structed. With additional handling of e.g., reused demolition 
materials, these become more expensive also related to the 
additional labor. In the demolition process, the electricity 
consumption has not been priced as this process is an expense 
of the contractor. 
Overall, the most expensive form of demolition is the 
complete demolition, however, the combined roof and façade 
is the most expensive form of demolition in relation to the 
area that is demolished, as illustrated in Figure 6. For the 
waste treatment option, the recycling treatment is the most 
expensive, which relates to the largest waste fraction being 
sent there. For the project revolving around the removal of 
polluted or hazardous substances, the landfilling process has 
the largest cost of the waste treatment options. The total cost 
per kg of waste managed is calculated, so that the cost of re-
cycling, incineration, landfill, and special treatment becomes 
approx. 500, 450, 2100, and 5600 DKK/ton of waste, respec-
tively. In this way, incineration and recycling are the cheapest 
waste treatment options, while the special treatment and land-
filling of waste have the largest cost per amount of waste. For 
many of the demolition projects, the combustible fractions 
were sent to the recycling. The reason for this can be to avoid 
the additional cost for person-hours of work as it is required

to downsize and divide the combustible elements to fit the

costly than sending combustibles to a recycling facility. 
The transport process has a higher cost compared to its 
overall contribution. This is because it was not possible to 
separate the cost of transporting materials and transporting 
waste. Overall, it is observed that the projects revolving 
around the removal of polluted and hazardous building ele-
ments were also the most expensive, while it is also the most 
environmentally harmful demolition in the CC and RD-m im-
pact category. There is no trend in the cost of the demolition 
practice and the construction year of the building. 
 
3.15 Comparison with 12 kg CO2-eq/m2/year 
To understand the results from this study in a larger per-
spective, the LCIA results were incorporated into the new leg-
islation of 12 kg CO2-eq/m2/year as well as compared to what 
was found in similar studies.  
As the average complete demolition practice (44.8 kg 
CO2-eq/m2) is divided by 50 years it almost contributes to an 
additional 1 kg CO2-eq/m2 annually for the CC impact on 
buildings. This effect is related to the current construction de-
sign and demolition practice as well as the energy grid. For a 
building practice that sets the stage for more recycling, e.g., 
design for disassembly or limited use of construction materi-
als with poor recycling properties, it is expected that the im-
pact from demolition is decreased while this would also con-
tribute to a more circular building practice (F. N. Rasmussen 
et al., 2019). For a change in the energy grid into a fossil free 
electricity mix, it is expected that the combustion of demoli-
tion waste would contribute to a burden as this is a more pol-
luting form of energy production compared to a non-combus-
tible process in the CC impact category (Kwon and 
Østergaard, 2012). The political goal for a fossil free future, 
eliminate the use of waste incineration long term, which could 
also improve the strategy and practice for more recycling and 
circularity in the construction sector.  
Furthermore, the construction year indicated that 11 (15 
including previous refurbishments) of the 26 demolition pro-
jects were refurbished within a 50year lifecycle while 4 were 
refurbished within 10 years. This is a result of stricter energy 
requirements and changes in the Danish Bygningsreglement, 
as well as poor construction practice and the use of hazardous 
substances which later require decontamination and lowers 
the recycling ability of the materials. When a refurbishment, 
from a combined roof and façade demolition, a hard strip, and 
decontamination (6.8 kg CO2-eq/m2), is included the impacts 
on CC have minimal effect on the new legislation of 12 kg 
CO2-eq/m2/year. Looking into each refurbishment, the pro-
jects containing metal scrap, except the soft strip demolition, 
were not refurbished within a 50year lifecycle. Recalculating 
the average of the combined roof and façade, soft strip, and 
decontamination without the environmental saving from the 
projects with steel substitution revealed an average of 20.3 kg 
CO2-eq/m2 which would add 0.41 kg CO2-eq/m2/year to the 
CC impact from the demolition practice on building LCAs.   
From the literature review, it was found that the demoli-
tion practice had little (0.99% (Le et al., 2021)) to no signifi-
cant impact on the LCA of buildings. The overall conclusions 
from this study do, however, reveal that the complete demo-
lition practice does affect up to 13% of the total building LCA 
relative to the measured (6.9 kg CO2-eq/m2/year from office 
buildings measured by Tozan et al. (2021)). From Tozan et 
al., 2021, a scenario-based calculation revealed that the refur-
bishment stage of the office buildings contributed to 7% of 
the overall CC impact. This amount to 0.48 kg CO2-
eq/m2/year which is very similar to the results modeled in this 
study. Only for two buildings, the overall building area (not 
limited to the demolished area) was below 1000 m2, and 
therefore the estimated results on refurbishments from the 
SBi report can be assumed to match the calculated results 
from this study.  
A change in the construction practice and design phase of 
buildings could significantly change the impacts on CC as it 
is found for the exclusion of the projects with the shredder 
fraction. For a reduction in the impact on CC on buildings, it 
is suggested to limit the variety of materials used in the design 
phase, so that the overall waste composition has fewer waste 
fractions, and so that the demolition timeframe becomes 
shorter. Furthermore, it is suggested to use material fractions 
which return a larger saving in its substitution processes such 
as any metal fraction and insulation. Crushing of heavy frac-
tions on site also contributes to a significant saving in 
transport and fuel consumption at the recycling facility.  
 
3.16 Limitation and robustness 
The overall conclusions of the LCIA are considered ro-
bust and reliable. The uncertainties regarding the electricity 
consumption could be expected to change the overall impacts 
from a demolition, however, the ranking between the demo-
lition subcategories would remain the same. A change in the 
construction practice with recycled or more biological mate-
rial options is expected to change the impact from the waste 
handling practice. With the recycling of a material, there is a 
slight chance that pollution can occur or that the element be-
comes less viable for recycling into a new production (substi-
tution ratios decrease) which leads to an increase in effects 
from the demolition and waste treatment practice.  
This LCA does not cover issues such as the spread of dust 
during demolition, site safety, noise, visual pollution, etc. Ad-
ditionally, this study did not include the effect from forecast-
ing of technologies such as a change in the electricity grid or 
an upscaling of the recycling and reuse in the demolition prac-
tice. The environmental impacts are not presented with tem-
poral resolution but rather total emissions, which can affect 
landfills where internal physical or chemical processes occur 
over long time periods. This study is also only provided for 
office buildings in Denmark. It could have been interesting to 
investigate a change in the LCIA results with a change in the 
building type. For more reliable demolition data, data collec-
tion should be done for more than one demolition company. 
From G. Tscherning, data for the number of days to conduct 
demolition, fuel consumption for machines, impurities for 
each waste fraction, and transport processes for equipment, 
materials, and employee commuting were all difficult to ob-
tain, and thus their reliability uncertain. Furthermore, the 
demolition cost and geographical representativeness are ex-
pected to change for the demolition practices outside of the 
capital region of Denmark, such as in Jutland or other islands.  
4. Conclusion 
This study illustrated the importance of including the 
demolition (C1-2) and associated avoided impacts (D), 
through an assessment of 6 different demolition subcatego-
ries. LCIA results revealed that the complete demolition is 
responsible for up to 13% of the CC impact on office build-
ings. When the demolition and refurbishment followed by 
waste treatment practice is included in the LCA of buildings 
the overall climate effect from the building could increase up 
==PAGE:15==
14  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
_________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
to 1.5 kg CO2-eq/m2/year for a 50year lifetime. The LCA of 
buildings could also be measured with a FU such as the man-
agement of demolition waste per largest waste fraction, how-
ever, this FU is best known after demolition. 
The overall results illustrated that the environmentally 
best demolition choice for CC would be the interior soft- and 
hard strip demolition, as they tend to have fewer heavy frac-
tions compared to other demolition subcategories. Here the 
projects with the largest variety in waste composition as well 
as the projects involving the removal of asbestos and hazard-
ous waste are the projects with the largest CC burden. For the 
hard strip demolition, it is worth noting that the variation in 
the results, due to a larger variety in the waste composition, 
contributes to more uncertain results. The worst type of dem-
olition to CC is the complete demolition which is also the 
demolition subcategory that contains the greatest total waste 
amount, largest variation in waste composition as well as the 
largest fraction of heavy fractions. From the contribution 
analysis, it is found that the demolition process share approx-
imately 40% of the total impact. Additionally, heavy fractions 
only contribute to a small saving in CO2-eq despite the heav-
ier waste amount, compared to metal scrap and insulation.  
Overall, the results illustrate how buildings with good re-
cyclable elements e.g., shredder, insulation, and to some ex-
tent gypsum, contribute to a substantial environmental saving 
for the CC impact category. Especially shredder is a sug-
gested building component because the substitution ratio is 
high and the recycling process well developed. It is also ad-
vantageous for the demolition process to have fewer waste 
fractions. It is not suggested to carry out a complete demoli-
tion, but rather transform the building and let the heavier 
building components remain. Lastly, it is important to obtain 
data on the actual electricity consumption for the demolition 
site accommodation, to ensure more reliable results for the 
most critical parameter (Demo_elec) for the modeling pro-
cess.  
For the RD-m impact category, the largest saving is cal-
culated for the hard strip and façade demolition subcatego-
ries. Overall, the results demonstrated that buildings with 
large quantities of shredder in the overall waste composition, 
contribute to a substantial environmental savings. The least 
advantageous demolition type is decontamination with no 
material substitution. For concrete and aggregate substitution, 
the impact saving is as low as 0.1%. This primarily illustrates 
that buildings with metal materials are better for RD-m com-
pared to other fractions. This also illustrates that the complete 
demolition subcategory with an excess of heavy fractions in 
the total waste composition has a larger waste quantity but 
with materials with less saving impacts. There was found no 
trend in either impact categories or the total waste amount per 
square area. Lastly, it is important to investigate the resulting 
effect of the RD-m with a different characterization factor. 
In general, no significant average value on RD-m saving 
can be found across the impact per demolished area. There is 
also no trend in how the demolition process impacts RD-m, 
but savings from the demolition activity could be obtained by 
limiting the period for site accommodation and the transport 
of waste and materials. Waste incineration contributes to a 
small environmental saving, while hazardous waste and land-
fills are processes that contribute little to the environmental 
effect in both impact categories. Lastly, the results for the nor-
malized values revealed that the demolition and following 
waste treatment process for a building impact CC by an aver-
age factor of 1000 greater than RD-m. Results could however 
vary, for the impacts found with a different mineral resource 
impact characterization and normalization unit.  
Overall, the most expensive form of demolition is the 
complete demolition, however, the combined roof and façade 
is the most expensive form of demolition in relation to the 
area that is demolished. Incineration and recycling are the 
cheapest waste treatment options, while the special treatment 
and landfilling of waste have the largest cost per amount of 
waste. The largest single activity cost is found for labor, 
which is also the least environmentally harmful “process” 
compared to electricity consumption, transport, and ma-
chines. Furthermore, the costly labor prevents sensitive care 
and cheap prices for the inclusion of reused materials in the 
construction sector.

1.0 Theory of LCA of building 
1.1 LCA of buildings according to EN15978:2011 
LCA of buildings differs from conventional LCA of products, as LCA of buildings requires a construc-
tion and demolition phase as well as a more detailed use phase included after the production of elements 
to the building. In this way, the entire building’s life cycle is considered holistically, and thus it can be 
determined which and how much parameters, building processes, or specific life cycle stages contribute 
to various environmental impacts or resource use (Christensen, 2011).  
The International Organization for Standardization (ISO) has created a process-based standard (ISO 
14040 and 14044) for LCA practitioners on any type of object. Meanwhile, the European EN 15978 is 
a more specific LCA standard method for buildings while EN1804 is an LCA standard for construction 
products (Rodriguez and Simonen, 2017)(Ngeow, 2020). The life cycle of a building is defined by its 
system boundary and must be divided into 5 stages and 17 subprocesses which can be seen in Figure 1. 
According to the EN15978:2011, the gains and drawbacks of recycling or reusing building waste are 
considered outside the system boundary and are thus required to be reported separately from the system 
(Trafik- og Byggestyrelsen, 2016).  
 
Figure 1 Building assessment information modules for LCA of buildings according to EN15804:2012 (Vilches et al., 2017)  
As found in the literature review none of the LCA of buildings included all stages and much less all 
subprocesses (see section 2.0 Literature review for clarification). Most often it is primarily the first 
module, the production stage (A1-3) that is best known. It is allowed to elude modules from an LCA if 
it is argued fairly. An example is the construction (A4-5) and demolition (C1-2) stages which are often 
referred to as negligible (Junnila and Horvath, 2003). If the LCA of the building is conducted during or 
right after the construction of the building, the use stage (B1-7), end-of-life stage (C1-4), and the ben-
efits from the recycling stage (D) are scenario-based which can be another argument from the exclusion 
of these modules (Trafik- og Byggestyrelsen, 2016). The service life, the period of time with which the 
building meets its initial requirement, of a building is also important as the environmental effects from 
materials will be either lower with a larger lifetime or vice versa. The service life additionally deter-
mines the number of replacements of construction products and the total operational energy use (Vilches 
et al., 2017). For better comparisons of LCA of buildings, the result must be calculated to the same unit, 
e.g., kg CO2-eq/m2/year, however, system boundaries, assumptions, and limitations still prevent good 
comparisons across studies (Rodriguez and Simonen, 2017).  
1.2 LCI databases and LCIA tools 
ISO 14044 brought standardization of the principle and system boundaries for LCA in general and for 
sector specific LCAs, however, the standard did not specify which LCIA method to apply for the cal-
==PAGE:20==
3  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
culations in practice. Furthermore, the environmental impacts from various processes are both geo-
graphical, temporal- and technological specific. Therefore, an increasing number of databases and 
LCIA methodologies have been under development (Hauschild and Huijbregts, 2015).  
Building construction, refurbishment, and waste management systems related to a building's end-of-life 
may utilize various chemicals, materials, and various kinds of energy, or produce secondary materials 
and ranges of energy products. The environmental process data for various processes and materials 
related to a building life cycle is required to be extracted from a database (Christensen, 2011). The data 
can be collected in a life cycle inventory (LCI) database, such as the ELCD database developed for the 
European Union (Fazio et al., 2015), the Swiss ecoinvent database (Frischknecht et al., 2005), GaBi 
database (Spatarb et al., n.d.), as well as multiple national databases better targeted for the environment 
and technological advancement of production and/or treatment options of the country and the national 
industry in question (Ciroth et al., 2019). Uncertainties follow the establishment of an inventory due to 
uncertainties in the raw data as well as the uncertainties following the model, the assumptions, and the 
extrapolation used to extend the coverage and consistency of the inventory. Examples could be the 
substance, the country or region, or the reference year considered for the inventory data (Laurent et al., 
2015). In the LCI each process consists of input- and output flows as illustrated in Figure 2 in the article, 
while the environmental exchange from the specific process is presented as a per functional output of 
the process, e.g., per kg for materials, per kWh electricity output for power plants, per kg km or m3 km 
for transportation processes, etc. Each database can be integrated with application software. Some of 
the most used LCA software tools today are GaBi (www.gabi-software.de), Umberto (www.um-
berto.de), SimaPro (www.pre.nl), and TEAM (www.ecobilan.com/uk team.php) (Christensen, 2011). 
The LCA tools can be used to perform life cycle impacts assessment (LCIA) calculations to translate 
inventory results to environmental impact results with the use of characterization factors (Ciroth et al., 
2019). A list of the various LCIA methodologies can be seen in Table 7. Limited methodologies exclu-
sively include the midpoint methods such that the indicator for a specific impact category is set for an 
intermediary point in the impact pathway, while others also include the endpoint method which directs 
the impacts to the end of the impacts pathway and divides the results into three subcategories: Human 
health, ecosystem quality and resource scarcity (Hauschild and Huijbregts, 2015).  
Table 7 LCIA methodologies for current best practice in LCA. Here o illustrates availability in the methodology but has not 
been further investigated in the ILCD Handbook, M and E are the availability of a midpoint- or endpoint model which have 
been further analyzed (European Commission, 2010a). The grey area is based on values from EASETECH and Google, not 
necessarily further analyzed.

methodologies, LCA tools and software are used to incorporate the results based on specific LCIA 
methodologies. Today, there is no Danish database (Trafik- og Byggestyrelsen, 2016), however, LCA-
Byg is a Danish software tool for LCA on buildings based on environmental product declarations 
(EPDs) from the Ökebaudat database. Other global LCA tools and software and their related LCIA

With the various tools, methodologies, and databases it is thus important to clearly define the choices 
for the calculations, etc. as required in the ISO 14044 and guided in the ILCD handbook (European 
Commission, 2010b).  
The ISO 14044 includes general and very flexible guidelines where allocation approaches are based on 
short-lived products, while e.g., the construction sector must incorporate more complex and long-lived 
products. The end-of-life treatment for these products could be incorporated with their individual ben-
efits and burdens of a shared material or component which should be allocated with methods for multi-
functionality (Christensen, 2011). The EN 15804 and EN 15978 specifically utilize the “cut-off”- at-
tributional allocation approach for which the impacts from both the production and recycling of a virgin 
product are attributed to the first use. The allocation method must not vary in the same LCA (Eberhardt 
et al., 2020). Other allocation approaches include substitution for which the multi-product activities are 
converted into single-product activities in a consequential model system and in this way the by-products 
are included as inputs with a negative sign in the system. System expansion will normally be feasible 
for waste management systems, however, for some product systems, this methodology may require too 
excessive and unnecessary data collection. In the consequential LCA, all multifunctionality processes 
are applied (Christensen, 2011).

2.0 Literature review 
LCAs have been applied to numerous studies related to the life cycle stages of buildings, but what key 
findings can be identified on how demolition is applied to these LCA practices, and what solutions for 
the inclusion of demolition are provided in the current research literature? To address these questions a 
critical review has been conducted on articles primarily targeting the LCA methodologies, type of allo-
cation, impact indicators, case-study boundaries, and gaps of knowledge for the construction, refurbish-
ment, demolition, and end-of-life waste management life cycle stages of buildings.  
A pre-systematic literature review was done based on searches in Web of Science and DTU FindIt. In 
total 731 studies were found containing words such as life-cycle assessment and demolition. The search 
was further narrowed to the specific life cycle stages of the building (construction, refurbishment, and 
waste management). It was chosen to exclude studies whose primary focus was on recycling building 
materials because these articles often focus on one material fraction rather than the entire building. The 
search terms and their respective article count is implied in Table 9 below. It should be noticed, that 
LCA articles for buildings that include the word demolition only account for 4% and 4.6% of all the 
articles for LCAs on buildings on each search engine, respectively.  
Table 9 Overview of search term results for the number of articles in English/Danish at DTU FindIt and Web of Science
