Only literature written in English or Danish was included to ensure a high international quality as well 
as temporal and spatial limitations related to the Danish geography and housing stock. Only papers 
including a practice in the life-cycle assessment were included. The literature search was further nar-
rowed by limiting the journal papers to exclude papers that deviated from the four main life-cycle stages 
of buildings. Lastly, a full text screening was done for 40 articles that had been chosen based on ab-
stracts and titles including words related to the search terms in Table 9 as well as certain articles ana-
lyzing the effect from the refurbishment of specific parts of buildings or construction. The reviewed 
articles were divided into subcategories: Construction (7), Demolition (6), Refurbishment (16), and 
Waste Management (7) while also four Review articles were included. The full list of articles can be 
seen in Table 12.  
2.1 Construction 
7 journal papers were united under the category “Construction” and reviewed for their practice of LCA 
on the building life cycle. The LCAs were either calculated in the design phase or right after the building 
had been constructed.  
Of the 7 articles, only 2 articles include demolition (C1) as part of the building life cycle. Both articles 
claim that the C1 process has little environmental impact. The first article by Junnila and Horvath (2003) 
argues that the construction (1.50%) and demolition (0.90%) process itself has a negligible impact on 
CC relative to the remaining life cycle stages (building materials (10.04%), use of building (84.15%), 
and maintenance (3.40%)) of a building. Le et al. (2021) state that the demolition and construction 
process account for 0.99% and 35.16% in design alternative 1 and 0.99% and 56.42% in design alter-
native 2 on CC, respectively. Both articles claim that the data availability for the C1 process as well as 
==PAGE:24==
7  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
systematically determined data relevant to the demolition practice were lacking. The problem of data 
availability on the construction (A5) and C1 process relates back to the life cycle stages of the EN15978 
standard which accept the exclusion of process stages. Furthermore, neither of the articles includes the 
benefits of recycling as the waste handling life-cycle stage is excluded.  
Le et al. (2021) specify that the primary source of energy to be used at the C1 stage was in the form of 
diesel from heavy machines like loaders, trucks, and excavators. On the contrary, Junnila, and Horvath 
(2003) do not specify which processes have been included or where the data has been collected in the 
LCA for both the A5 and the C1 practice. Here the LCA study is made in the design phase before the 
final building structure was known and was thus based on plans, while Le et al. (2021), is calculated 
when the building has just started its operation phase with known construction materials. The differ-
ences and similarities between the two articles including demolition can be seen in Table 10.

The C1 process was not included in the remaining studies due to a lack of data (Koroneos et al., 2015), 
an excluded process in EPDs (Tozan et al., 2021), and energy and environmental impacts are from 
human resources employed (Giordano et al., 2021), or the process was simply not mentioned (Andro-
sevic et al., 2019). Of the 40 papers reviewed in total, only two studies include a reference to why 
demolition is not applied. With the snowballing tool it is found that the same reference is utilized (Tar-
antini et al., 2009), however, this article does not include a reference to why demolition is excluded. 
Waste management (C3-4) was not mentioned in any of the construction articles, despite what was 
indicated in the title “Life Cycle Assessment as a Tool for Low Waste Building Systems, Case Study 
Building External Wall” by Androsevic et al. (2019).  
The LCA methodologies applied to calculate the life cycle of the buildings varied from EURECA 
(Giordano et al., 2021) to a hybrid approach combining both input-output and LCA in the early design 
==PAGE:25==
8  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
phase (Le et al., 2021). Athena Impact Estimator is used for buildings in North America (Azari and 
Badri, 2021) while LCA-Byg is used for general building types in Denmark (Tozan et al., 2021). Koro-
neos et al. (2015) use the GaBi Software together with the CML2001 normalization factors while Jun-
nila and Horvath (2003) utilize the KCL-ECO 3.0 life cycle indicator program, both for office buildings. 
Generally, this illustrates that a lack of standard reporting of LCA results for buildings limits the repro-
ducibility and comparability of LCA studies. Furthermore, neither of the articles mentions the allocation 
method applied.  
Additional limitations and gaps of knowledge were found in the individual articles. It is mentioned that 
Environmental Product Declarations (EPDs) are limited to projects based in countries that provide 
EPDs and can only be conducted in the final design stage (Giordano et al., 2021). It is also mentioned 
that the early design stage lacks design details and has difficulty exploring design alternatives (Azari 
and Badri, 2021). The research also primarily focuses on 20 main materials, such as ready mixed con-
crete, reinforcement steel, structural steel, sand, aggregate, cement, cement mortar, bricks, galvanized 
steel, plasterboard, glass, paint, ceramic tiles, marble stone, wood, PVC, steel doors, MDF (doors), 
timber (doors), and aluminum framed (doors and windows). Given that these materials are the load-
bearing elements of the building the quantities for these materials can be easily extracted (Le et al., 
2021). Building systems such as HVAC, electricity, structural, and lighting are rarely included and for 
office buildings in particular inventory furniture, manufacturing of construction equipment, and con-
struction of infrastructure were excluded (Junnila and Horvath, 2003). Furthermore, it is difficult to 
determine the operation stage from the design stage as well as the service lifetime and the proportions 
of the individual building components that need to be replaced (Le et al., 2021).  
2.2 Demolition 
6 journal papers were united under the category “Demolition” and reviewed for their inclusion of C1 in 
the practice of LCA on the building life cycle. Here similar gaps were found in the LCA practice for 
the reviewed articles in the subcategory “Construction.” Here it is also stated that the integrated life 
cycle inventory data on the foreground dismantling and demolition process is less comprehensive 
(Bozhilova-Kisheva et al., 2012; Ivanica et al., 2022).  
All 6 journal papers include the C3-4 process in the life cycle stage; however, one study excluded the 
waste composition as it was primarily focused on the LCA of asbestos in buildings (Kim et al., 2016). 
The remaining five studies include between 4 fractions (steel, red brick, concrete, and mixed waste) 
(Bozhilova-Kisheva et al., 2012) and up to 14 waste fractions (Concrete, steel bars, bricks, mortar, 
plaster, paint, mineral insulation, wood, glass, ceramics, roof tiles, plastic (PVC), aluminum, copper) 
(Blengini, 2009). In this way, the composition of waste differs from being clearly defined to being 
greatly assumed. Apart from Liu et al. (2020), who classify the specific waste fractions into A (recycling 
and landfill), B (recycled aggregates and powder), and C (landfill) categories, neither of the remaining 
five articles specify which waste fraction is treated with the different waste handling methodologies 
analyzed. With a specification of the specific waste fractions as well as their respective treatment meth-
ods the studies become more comparable. In general studies on demolition waste include clean and 
heavy waste fractions but exclude the removal of pollutants such as asbestos contained materials (Kim 
et al., 2016). 
Specifically for the waste treatment processes, it is mentioned that while eco balances of recovery and 
landfill plants cannot be obtained the production of biogas and leachate should also be neglected for the 
characterization of 95% of inert waste in the total landfilled solid waste (Sára et al., 2001). The envi-
ronmental effects related to landfilling are context-specific and vary significantly with waste composi-
tion, geographic placement, and technological advancement. It is therefore recommended to adopt a 
case-specific approach (Laurent et al., 2014). Neither study exemplify how the landfill process has been 
modeled, the timeframe used, the boundaries related to long-term emissions, etc., and thus again com-
parisons can be difficult to obtain.  
==PAGE:26==
9  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
Related process costs, safety, and health of workers contribute to the influence of the quality of the 
demolition materials which determine the suitability of the materials for increased or high-grade recy-
cling (Bozhilova-Kisheva et al., 2012). It is also important to include the insurmountable limits to the 
recycling process such as the loss of mass, the energy consumption, decay of quality, and pollution. 
Blengini (2009), therefore, suggests that the correct solution lies between the conventional and second-
ary quarries. 
The LCA methodology is applied with various simulation tools such as input-output tables (Bozhilova-
Kisheva et al., 2012), GaBi software with the LCIA method ReCiPe (H) 2016 v1.1 (Ivanica et al., 2022), 
and the EcoIndicator 99 with SimaPro 6 and Boustead Model 5 as supporting tools (Blengini, 2009). 
Liu et al. (2020) utilize carbon emission factors for diesel to 4.16 kg CO2eq/kg from IPCC 2013 GWP 
100a V1.01 (Wang et al., 2018) and 0.59 for electricity (Luo, 2016). Kim et al. (2016) exclusively 
utilize the IPCC GHG calculation method for both the transportation and removal processes. One study 
completely excludes the LCIA method or software applied (Sára et al., 2001). Furthermore, because the 
life cycle of demolition waste is short it has not been relevant to include the time value of money in the 
LCC (Liu et al., 2020). As with the journal articles for “Construction” comparisons of results for LCA 
studies which include demolition can be difficult to achieve. Also, no allocation method has been spec-
ified for either of the 6 journal papers.  
2.3 Refurbishment 
16 journal papers were united under the category “Refurbishment” and reviewed for their practice of 
LCA on the building life cycle. The studies were further divided into comparisons of refurbishment and 
demolition (6) and analyses of various retrofitting scenarios (10).  
Here it is found that LCAs of buildings primarily focus on the analysis of new efficient buildings thus 
neglecting the existing building stock. In addition to this, the studies dealing with the B4-5 stage of 
buildings only include environmental gains from the existing building and therefore not with the new 
equivalent construction (Marique and Rossi, 2018). Limited studies evaluate the diesel burned and en-
ergy needed for the A5, B4-5, and C1 of the buildings (Ghose et al., 2019; Itard and Klunder, 2007; 
Pomponi et al., 2015; Rabani et al., 2021; Weiler et al., 2017). Supplementary to this, individual build-
ing elements and materials are assessed for their environmental impact respectively, however, their 
combination and the material choice effect of the A4-5, B1, B4, operational use (B6-7), and C3-4 stage 
is rarely assessed (Rønning et al., n.d.). Typically, the transportation distances are contextualized and 
normalized to the country in focus (Nematchoua et al., 2019). Some studies do not incorporate the 
surrounding environment for the building and thus neglect obstacles in both the A5 and C1 phase (Öster-
bring et al., 2019).  
Results from studies that are linked to their respective building/case study, geographical location, and 
function, cannot be directly transferred to other buildings or cities (Itard and Klunder, 2007; Weiler et 
al., 2017). Setting the functional unit per person or square meter greatly improves the comparability 
(Nematchoua et al., 2019). With known values of building area, it should be possible to recalculate the 
impacts to a comparable level, however, one should still be cautious with the building types, geograph-
ical and temporal scope as well as technological advancement of waste treatment options (Laurent et 
al., 2014). Further research is also sought after for the inclusion of a wider range of natural building 
materials in the LCA databases (Ruggieri et al., 2013).  
7 articles declare that the C1 stage is included as part of the life cycle, however, the data inventory and 
the practice are missing (Berg and Fuglseth, 2018; Ghose et al., 2019; Pittau et al., 2019; Rønning et 
al., n.d.; Weiler et al., 2017) or only included as a transportation (C2) process from the demolition site 
to the treatment plants and landfills (Petrovski et al., 2020). One study included the practice of demoli-
tion as a factor of time and vehicles used (Marique and Rossi, 2018) while the most detailed study 
included a resource quantification bank consisting of disaggregated construction units divided into labor 
hours, machinery hours, and mass of materials (Alba-Rodríguez et al., 2017). The LCIA tool varied 
==PAGE:27==
10  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
from undefined (Eaton and Amato, 1998; Rønning et al., n.d.), national software tools (Weiler et al., 
2017) to the CML impact assessment method (Ghose et al., 2019; Marique and Rossi, 2018), ReCiPe 
Midpoint characterization (Berg and Fuglseth, 2018) and the Ecological Footprint (EF) and Carbon 
Footprint (CF) indicator (Alba-Rodríguez et al., 2017). Of the remaining 9 studies that excluded the 
demolition process, three of these also utilize the ReCiPe hierarchic perspective midpoint (Österbring 
et al., 2019; Pittau et al., 2019; Pomponi et al., 2015), thus indicating that the studies utilizing the ReC-
iPe have greater chances of a fair comparison across studies.  
The choice of allocation methods was not defined for the analyzed journal papers in the subcategories 
"Construction" and “Demolition.” In the subcategory “Refurbishment” the allocation method has been 
specified for five articles. The cut-off method which implies that the initial production of a material is 
allocated to the primary use of the material is utilized by Rabani et al. (2021) and Weiler et al. (2017). 
The attributional approach, however, not specified for the use of cut-off or allocation-at-the-point-of-
substitution methods, is utilized by Pittau et al. (2019) and Pomponi et al. (2015), while the consequen-
tial approach was utilized by Ghose et al. (2019). Both the attributional and consequential allocation 
method was utilized for the ReCiPe LCIA methodology, while the cut-off method was utilized with 
Umberto and Norwegian EPDs, illustrating that the cut-off method is utilized for the nationally specific 
studies while EPDs, by the law of the EN 15804 and EN 15978, are required to use the cut-off allocation 
methodology. With the cut-off and unspecific attributional allocation method both being used twice it 
thus illustrates that this allocation type is preferable to the consequential allocation approach. The type 
of allocation is steered by the software tools used, however, even though the cut-off allocation method 
is chosen as the main approach for LCA on buildings it is not found to be mentioned in various LCAs. 
For the ReCiPe LCIA methodology, it is established that the attributional and consequential allocation 
approach is preferred.  
Of the 16 studies, only four include C4-5 (Berg and Fuglseth, 2018; Eaton and Amato, 1998; Nematch-
oua et al., 2019; Pittau et al., 2019) while of these studies only one specifies the waste composition, 
however still in general material fractions such as 75.6% being inert waste materials (Pittau et al., 2019). 
Overall, this illustrates that waste management is significantly neglected in the study of LCA for build-
ings and can illustrate larger impacts for studies that include updated data for the case study in question.  
2.4 Waste management 
7 journal papers were united under the category “Waste management” and reviewed for their practice 
of LCA on the building life cycle.  
Similarly, to the studies in the previous subcategories, only a few studies include C1 as part of the life-
cycle system. Of the four studies which did include the demolition practice only two specified the dem-
olition practice. Here impacts from both the machinery activities, C2 of waste, and embodied emissions 
with the demolition waste were included in the journal article of Wang et al. (2022a), while Plebankie-
wicz et al. (2019) also included the cost of building disposal, equipment, and machinery as well as land 
use and additional assets. Brogaard and Christensen (2016) only included waste transport while Zam-
brana-Vasquez et al. (2016) did not define the practice at all. This trend, of utilizing different methods 
for incorporating the demolition practice follows the same trend seen in the previously analyzed sub-
categories and makes comparisons across studies difficult. 
Interestingly to the title of the subcategory, two studies excluded the waste composition analyzed (Bro-
gaard and Christensen, 2016; Plebankiewicz et al., 2019), while one study only examined the LCA of 
concrete waste (Guignot et al., 2015). The remaining articles include waste fractions such as waste 
concrete, stones, and crushed stones, soil and dust, bricks, bamboo and wood, metals, asphalt, glass, 
textile, and other organic matter (Butera et al., 2015; Wang et al., 2022a) as well as mixed and reject 
fractions (Mercante et al., 2012). Again, these studies neglect to inform which waste fractions are sent 
to which waste treatment facility, while the technology of the various waste treatment options only has 
been vaguely described. Zambrana-Vasquez et al. (2016) include the composition of waste based on the 
==PAGE:28==
11  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
building components such as an internal wall, internal foundation, ground foundation, roof, glasses, 
vertical structure, and electrical apparatus. The waste treatment choice of either waste fraction has not 
been specified, but the type of materials for each building component is a good differentiation method 
to determine the effects of waste management from the various building components. It is further men-
tioned that the largest limitation to the studies is the waste composition and their respective recycling 
potential (Butera et al., 2015; Wang et al., 2022b). 
The LCIA method varies from the ReCiPe MidPoint method (Guignot et al., 2015; Wang et al., 2022b), 
the CML 2002 (Mercante et al., 2012), the ILCD midpoint (Butera et al., 2015), and various other 
national software and EPD databases. Only Brogaard and Christensen (2016) include the consequential 
approach with the ReCiPe LCIA methodology, such that the marginal production of energy and mate-
rials (also from infrastructure included as capital goods) is substituted into their respective relevant 
scenario. This aligns with the theory of utilizing the consequential approach for waste management 
systems as described in 1.2 LCI databases and LCIA tools. The allocation modeling approach is not 
mentioned in the remaining articles. This also resembles the informational gap in the LCIA methodol-
ogy as described in the previous subcategory reviews.

An overview of the reviewed journal articles can be seen in Table 11 above. Furthermore, only 1 of the 
40 reviewed studies include the impacts from capital goods, and thus an inclusion of capital goods 
would decrease the comparison potentials for the studies.  
The impact categories analyzed in all the reviewed articles vary from solely including the global warm-
ing potential (Berg and Fuglseth, 2018; Eaton and Amato, 1998; Kim et al., 2016; Le et al., 2021; Liu 
et al., 2020; Marique and Rossi, 2018; Pomponi et al., 2015; Rabani et al., 2021; Rønning et al., n.d.; 
Weiler et al., 2017; Zambrana-Vasquez et al., 2016), to differentiating the impacts in 1. Non-toxicity 
related impact categories: Global Warming, Stratospheric Ozone Depletion, Ionizing Radiation (Human 
health), Photochemical ozone Formation, Freshwater Eutrophication, Marine Eutrophication, Terres-
trial Acidification, Terrestrial Eutrophication, Particulate Matter, 2. Toxicity related impact categories: 
Human Toxicity (Cancer Effect), Human Toxicity (non-Cancer Effect), Freshwater Ecotoxicity, and 3. 
Resource impact categories: Depletion of Abiotic Resources - Fossil, Depletion of Abiotic Resources - 
Elements (Brogaard and Christensen, 2016; Cetiner and Ceylan, 2013; Ghose et al., 2019; Wang et al., 
2022b). Only one article additionally included land use in the wide range of impact categories (Pittau 
et al., 2019), while another also included odors (Nematchoua et al., 2019). This aligns with the various 
choices of software tools to calculate the LCAs of buildings. For the best comparison practice, it is 
found to incorporate the 17 impacts categories and assign them to their respective endpoint indicator as 
possible for the ReCiPe hierarchic perspective midpoint impact assessment methodology.  
The methodology for the inclusion of the C1 activity stage varies significantly, however for best results 
on the data quality assessment and most complete LCA study it is suggested to include collected data 
from the demolition activity. The demolition activity should include energy consumption, fuel for trans-
portation and machinery as well as additional resources, which can vary for each respective case study. 
==PAGE:29==

      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
It is also suggested that future studies include additional measurements and factors such as fine partic-
ulate matter emissions, particle sizes, soil contamination (Ivanica et al., 2022), as well as quantitative 
indicators for the use of land with the avoidance of landfilling (Blengini, 2009). Powder and noise 
emissions from demolition itself have thus far been excluded in the LCA (Sára et al., 2001). Neither 
will be included as the data availability is too unattainable for this study.

3.0 Modelling framework 
3.1 The LCA approach 
The common guidance from the ILCD handbook which conforms to the ISO14044:2006 is followed to 
conduct a robust, consistent, and quality assured life cycle assessment study. This study incorporates 
the LCA practice by dividing the project into four sections: 1) Goal and Scope, 2) Life Cycle inventory 
(LCI), 3) Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) and 4) Life Cycle Interpretation (European Commis-
sion, 2010b). 
Here the goal and scope are based on a literature review assessing the gaps of knowledge within the 
problem domain and the categorized demolition and waste management themes. In the LCI the in- and 
outputs of the entire system are introduced with data collection from the demolition company G. 
Tscherning A/S. The results are presented in the LCIA as characterized and normalized values. Finally, 
the Interpretation phase is used to backtrack, troubleshoot, and incorporate the previous sections to 
assemble the assessment to follow the initial purpose. The interpretation also includes the identification 
of significant issues such as the most relevant impact categories and processes as well as their influence 
on the results by utilizing a sensitivity analysis and with the incorporation of completeness and uncer-
tainty check. The conclusions can be extracted from the interpretation and done for the overall study.  
 
3.2 Goal definition 
3.2.1 Intended application of the results 
The intended application of this study is to investigate the potential environmental impacts related to 
various demolition activities and their associated end-of-life waste treatment process. The results can 
be used in broadening the perspective of a full LCA on buildings where C1-C2 stages are included.  
3.2.2 Method assumption and impact limitations 
The model is limited by the availability of data, while e.g., impurities are excluded from the source 
separated fractions, and lack of primary Danish data for the waste treatment process data obtained from 
the ecoinvent database. It is assumed that all source separated fractions are clean without impurities 
except for the mixed waste fractions separated into mixed waste categories 1 and 2, as described further 
in section 3.4. This also explains the absence of residues in the respective waste treatment options.  
Furthermore, the study is site specific for Danish demolition- and waste treatment activities, and thus, 
the results will be limited and unsuited for location applications outside the system boundary until 
proven significantly similar. The boundaries for this study include the niche market of demolition 
while the construction of the buildings and construction waste are excluded. 
Capital goods have been excluded from the study to obtain better comparison results as determined in 
the literature review. 
3.2.3 Reasons and decision context for conducting the study 
Today LCA of buildings is usually calculated by excluding the A4-5 stages and C1-C2 stages process 
due to insufficient data availability (Gervasio and Dimona, 2018; Koroneos et al., 2015). With this study 
specifically focusing on the C1 activity on office buildings, it will be possible to obtain and include 
more correct and realistic results on the demolition activity for a refurbishment and in this way also 
include additional life cycle data on building elements with a shorter lifetime than the building itself. 
Furthermore, the information regarding the environmental impacts can be used to pressure politicians 
and lawmakers in determining a better framework for environmental mitigation. Especially for the im-
pact on CC, this study could provide an extension to the new political legislation of 12 kgCO2eq/m2/year 
currently based on A1-3, B4, B6, and C3-4 for new buildings.  
==PAGE:37==
20  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
By excluding the decisions for which the LCAs are based and thus neglecting the large-scale conse-
quences of a change in the demolition- and waste treatment practice on the background system the de-
cision context falls within situation A - micro-level decision support. 
3.2.4 Target audience 
The target audience of the deliverables is the demolition company, G. Tscherning A/S, the Department 
of Industrial Ecology from the Norwegian University of Science and Technology, and the Department 
of Environmental Engineering from the Technical University of Denmark. Both institutions have 
knowledge of the LCA of waste management as well as a long history of developing MFA modeling to 
determine energy savings resulting from a refurbishment of a national building stock. 
In addition to this, the results have been intended for the public and the appendix is also going to be 
readable for all audiences.  
3.2.5 Comparative studies disclosed to the public 
Neither a systematic nor a product specific comparison will be made in this study. This study will in-
stead include a comparative assertion in the form of providing results from an LCA on buildings that 
both include and exclude the industry-specific process of demolition activities. The comparative asser-
tion is planned to be disclosed to the public which entails several additional mandatory requirements as 
determined in the ISO 14040 and 14044:2006.  
3.2.6 Commissioner of the study and other influential actors 
This study has been conducted as a 30 ECTS-point Master thesis for a Double master’s degree between 
the Technical University of Denmark and the Norwegian University of Science and Technology. The 
goal and scope of the study have been defined in collaboration with the supervisors of each university. 
The data collection is from the demolition company G. Tscherning. The main author, responsible for 
the LCA modeling and interpretation, was a student assistant at G. Tscherning. It should be noted that 
no economic exchanges have been made regarding this project. The actors influenced or influencing 
this study have been listed below:  
Technical University of Denmark: 
Maria Preilev Hansen (main author, student at DTU and NTNU) 
Anders Damgaard (Main Supervisor, Senior Researcher at DTU) 
Norwegian University of Science and Technology: 
Helge Brattebø (Co supervisor, Senior Researcher at NTNU) 
G. Tscherning A/S: 
Thomas Willumsen (External supervisor, chief of demolition at G. Tscherning)  
 
3.3 Scope definition 
3.3.1 Deliverables 
The deliverables of the study will include LCI- and LCIA results. These will be presented and inter-
preted in a comparative assertion life cycle assessment study provided in an article with supplementary 
material.  
3.3.2 Objective of the assessment 
The LCA case study is a real-size systematic comparative assessment of a wide range of the environ-
mental impacts used to highlight the hotspots related to refurbishment and the main difference in the 
==PAGE:38==
21  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
effect from each refurbishment stage on office buildings in Denmark. The demolition activities, namely 
roof- and/or façade renovation, decontamination from environmentally hazardous substances, interior 
soft- and hard stripping, and complete demolition are used to represent the various spectrum of demo-
lition activities in Denmark. Here a roof and façade demolition are the removal of a roof and façade, 
respectively, a soft strip demolition is a partial removal of the interior of a building, and a hard strip 
demolition is the demolition of a building where the structural building frame remains, complete dem-
olition is the complete removal of a building, and decontamination is the removal of individual hazard-
ous materials. 
The functional unit is the management of 1 m2 of demolition waste for six different demolition subcat-
egories in Denmark. It was also chosen to investigate the environmental impacts with a functional unit 
of managing demolition waste per largest waste fraction, managing demolition waste per working day, 
managing demolition waste per cost, and managing demolition waste per person-hours of work for the 
project. With these units, it will be possible to identify which parameter is the most average and include 
parameters in each subcategory. The LCIA results should then be included in the LCA of other buildings 
to better document the impact of a full building LCA with realistic lifetime changes of elements.

The reference flow varies for each project and demolition activity. The reference flow can be seen in 
Table 13. A further explanation of each demolition project can be found in section 4.0 Life cycle inven-
tories as well as individual waste compositions. 
3.3.3 LCI modeling framework and handling of multifunctional processes 
According to EN 15978, the LCA standard method for this study should be and is based on the attribu-
tional cut-off modeling (Rodriguez and Simonen, 2017). Furthermore, multifunctional processes are 
managed according to the recommendations from the ISO14044:2006 by subdivision or system expan-
sion. Marginal processes were used for the modeling of the background system, as recommended by 
the ILCD Handbook (European Commission, 2010a). 
3.3.4 System boundaries and completeness requirements 
The LCIA in this study is solely based on the C1-C4 stages of the EN 15978. The waste treatment 
processes included in the study are controlled landfills, material recycling for dry waste materials, spe-
cial treatment of hazardous waste, and Waste-to-Energy (WtE). The demolition process includes the 
consumption of electricity and water, fuel for machinery, employee commuting, and delivery of mate-
rials. The construction process and the upstream processes of material production have been excluded 
from the assessment.  
In agreement with the goal definition of the study, the cut-off method is utilized to investigate the allo-
cation of waste from one end-use to another. As recommended by the ISO 14044:2006 for systems in 
situation A average data (e.g., marginal Danish average mix based on natural gas for electricity and 
solely natural gas for heating) is used for electrical and thermal energy. Material recovery is included 
through system expansion with different substitution factors depending on the recycling technology and 
quality of the secondary product, for each waste fraction to substitute the virgin materials. This is mod-
eled such that, the inventory for the reuse-specific functions is isolated in the system while all other 
waste treatment flows are considered non-functional product flows. Figure 2 provides a typical illustra-
tion of how the modeling will be performed which will be more specific for each demolition project. 
 
Figure 2 Overview of a typical model system for the demolition subcategories. 
3.3.5 Representativeness of LCI data 
Geographical representativeness 
The geographical scope is Denmark. The waste fractions are generated on the demolition site within the 
system boundaries. Data collection is done from the Danish demolition company G. Tscherning (waste 
generation, demolition activities, waste transportation) and Danish waste treatment facilities (potential 
utilization of clean waste fractions from Materials Recovery Facilities (MRF), WtE incineration, land-
fills, and special waste treatment incineration). The placement of the demolition site and the waste 
handling facility chosen has been obtained and calculated based on distances found on Google Maps.  
==PAGE:40==
23  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
Temporal representativeness 
From the demolition company various waste budgets have been given for:  
• 
3 complete demolitions 
• 
5 hazardous decontaminations 
• 
6 soft strips demolitions 
• 
5 hard strips demolitions 
• 
1 façade demolition (with only concrete as a waste fraction) 
• 
3 roof demolitions 
• 
3 combined façade and roof demolitions 
The projects spanned from 2018 to 2022. Furthermore, data have been provided on the fuel consump-
tion, energy use, internal transportation, working hours, the total number of truckloads from the demo-
lition site, and weight-based truckloads from the first material treatment facility to the final End-Of-
Life treatment. The total accounting for each of the individual projects has also been provided to form 
a cost estimate in each demolition subcategory. The recycling percentages from the recycling facilities 
have also been found in the RGS sustainability report from 2020 (RGS Nordic, 2020) and interviews 
with waste handler Christa Engell from Dansk Miljøforbedring (2022). The data in this study is assumed 
to represent conditions from 2018 to 2025.  
Technological representativeness 
The material fractions, treatment processes, and chemical composition have been collected from the 
ecoinvent database version 2.7.1. The transport processes and the treatment processes for recycling as 
well as the landfilling, special treatment of hazardous waste, reprocessing, and substitution of materials 
have been chosen for the country of Switzerland due to the lack of inventory data from Denmark. It is 
assumed that the technological processes are similar to the best practice in Denmark. From the EA-
SETECH database, the process of incineration has been chosen as it fully represents Danish treatment 
processes. For each process and parameter, a uniform or normalized distribution has been established 
to incorporate all uncertainties. The efficiency of the various treatment facilities is related to the specific 
waste handler. A data quality assessment has been made for each utilized process and suggested param-
eters to obtain a better understanding of the data representativeness, completeness, and reliability.  
3.3.6 Preparation of the basis for the impact assessment 
The model is generated with the use of waste and energy data for 26 different demolition projects from 
G. Tscherning A/S. The software used is EASETECH (Clavreul et al., 2014).  
All impact categories are found with the same LCIA methodology: ReCiPe MidPoint 2016 method with 
the hierarchic perspective. Long-term effects are excluded from the study. In 2013 Hauschild et al. 
(2013) identified the ReCiPe method for CC and RD-m as the best resulting endpoint models. Further-
more, it was found that both methods were also classified as immature for recommendation similar to 
other LCIA models. This study was based on the ReCiPe MidPoint 2008 method and therefore it is 
expected that the LCIA results from the utilization of the ReCiPe 2016 method will be more precise. 
CC and RD-m are the impact categories that will be in focus during the interpretation of the LCIA 
results. It is still important to be observant of the resulting limitation on the midpoint characterization. 
The impact categories water use and land use have been excluded from the study as these impact cate-
gories are limited to location specific impact categories. Fossil resource depletion has also been ex-
cluded as there cannot be obtained normalized results for this impact category.  
The results from the impact categories are aggregated into normalization factors and abbreviated as 
illustrated in Table 1 in the article to compare the output related to the different demolition activities. It 
was not chosen to implement a weighted ranking of which demolition activity had the largest negative 
environmental effects.

is part of a Master thesis evaluation the reviewers will be the examinators.

4.0 Life cycle inventories 
The LCI includes both data collection and calculation of input- and output flows of the life cycle. Sub-
sequent to the demolition process is the transport of waste to various waste treatment facilities. This 
section provides an overview of the multiple LCI data used for the LCAs. The processes will be intro-
duced in the subsections as described below. Notice, that each process has been described in detail, and 
it is not necessary to read about each process, view table specifications, or model process in EA-
SETECH unless additional information about a specific project is needed. Thus, it is possible to read 
and understand the first R-1 process to understand the setup of the 25 remaining demolition projects. 
1. Roof demolition 
2. Façade demolition 
3. Interior soft strip demolition  
4. Interior hard strip demolition 
5. Complete demolition 
6. Decontamination 
The inventory data included in the models in EASETECH are either based on inventory data already 
existing in the EASETECH database or EcoSpold data extracted from the ecoinvent database.  
The material fractions are either taken directly from EASETECH or modified from a combination of 
similar waste categories. The waste fractions and their chemical composition can be seen in the Excel 
Appendix Overview sheet Material fractions. An example is the waste fraction Mixed waste (Category 
2) which is a mix of combustibles, wood, glass, shredder, plastic, gypsum, and insulation (category 1 
mixed waste does not include insulation and gypsum materials). Furthermore, the recycling and substi-
tution percentages are defined as parameters with a uniform distribution. The recycling percentages 
primarily follow the trend from the recycling facility RGS Nordic. Here, the electricity and fuel con-
sumption from RGS Nordic account for 0.00256 kWh and 0.0067 l per kg of total wet waste (RGS 
Nordic, 2020). Due to the majority (84%) of the fuel consumption being caused by the operations of 
machines (shredders, soil screeners, loaders, materials sorters, etc.) no additional processes from ecoin-
vent have been found for the crushing of heavy fractions. An overview of the parameters and their 
respective distribution can be found in Table 14. The sensitivity analysis numbers are the maximum 
and minimum values for the uniform distribution and the average value and the standard deviation for 
the normal distribution.

For the modeling of the incineration process, it was chosen to use a Danish standard process already 
integrated into EASETECH, while the energy efficiencies were chosen for Amager Resource Center 
with an electricity recovery up to 24.5% and a heat recovery of 62.4% from the lower heating value of 
the waste (Bisinella et al., 2022) These energy efficiencies are also given a uniform distribution, as 
illustrated in Table 14. Additionally, the electricity mix is an imported Danish marginal mix, primarily 
from natural gas.  
Three landfill processes have been imported from ecoinvent. A better and more combined process, in 
EASETECH, could have been used, however since the landfilling process is similar in each project the 
simpler model did not influence the scope of the results. The process for special treatment with energy 
recovery has also been imported from ecoinvent.

Roof demolition R-1 
Project R-1 is the largest roof demolition of 11 buildings with a total roof area of 4223 m2. The demo-
lition included the removal of roof tiles including the mortar (underlining), roof hoods, roof windows, 
roof battens, roofing felt, stern boards, gutters, downspouts, pipe holders, footplates, gutter iron, twig 
zinc, a full stripping of rafters, as well as removal of cladding on twigs into the post construction. Beams 
on twigs are also removed, as well as walkways. Lastly, removal and disposal of existing insula-
tion/granules in roof spaces, pointed ceilings, and sloping roof surfaces were included in the demolition 
contract.  
Pollution occurs in the form of lead, as well as soot from the demolition of the chimneys. Asbestos was 
found as dusty pipe bends as well as in the removal of expansion vessels on the ceilings. The project 
specific included processes for the modeling can be seen in Figure 3 and Table 16. The building is from 
1964 and the project spanned from the 2nd of June 2020 to the 15th of March 2021, which amount to 266 
days (including weekends). The project also required a total of 5385.5 person-hours of work.  
 
1 Plaster waste is the fraction of plaster ceilings removed from the renovation of older buildings as well as the 
excessive waste from a sandblasting such as light masonry from heavy building elements.

Roof demolition R-2 
Project R-2 is the smallest of the roof demolitions. The roof consists of fiber cement (slate eternity) 
with asbestos. In addition, the roof kit contains both fiber cement and PAHs, which were handled as 
asbestos. The roofing felt also had to be removed. The project specific processes for the modeling of 
the roof demolition are similar to that of project R-1 but with a variation in waste flows and input, output 
parameters, transportation distances, etc. The processes can be seen in Figure 4 and Table 17 below.  
This project spanned from the 20th of May to the 15th of July 2020 - a project period of 56 days (including 
weekends). The building was constructed in 1906 and the total area of the roof is found to be 86.4 m2. 
The project also required a total of 1182.5 person-hours of work.

Roof demolition R-3 
The roof demolition for project R-3 is a third kind of roofing material. Here the project entails the 
replacement of aluminum roofs on four buildings, approximately 1549 m2 in total. The aluminum roof 
consists of 0.7 mm folded aluminum including joints, sicoral cardboard with tar substances and nickel, 
25x127 mm rough-hewn boards, 2x50x127 mm barrier construction on leveling pieces, and 100 mm 
==PAGE:48==
31  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
insulation. Additionally, the project includes the demolition and disposal of existing flashings, fascia 
edges, and total drainage systems. One of the buildings also has a passage with a glass roof which must 
be demolished, while another building has an additional layer of insulation. The EASETECH modeled 
processes can be seen in Figure 5 and Table 18. 
The building is from 1970 and the project spanned from the 25th of May to the 5th of August 2020 with 
a project period of 72 days (including weekends) divided into 565.5 total person-hours of work.

4.2 Facade demolition 
It was difficult to obtain data on a façade demolition exclusively, however it was possible to obtain data 
on a combination of a roof and façade demolition which have been included in this section. Data have 
been provided for one façade demolition alone (F-4 which only constitutes the removal of concrete) 
and for three combination projects (RF-1 which was the removal of asbestos roof and lots of shredder, 
RF-2 which was the removal of a brick wall, and RF-3 which was the average removal of a cement wall 
and roofing felt) from the demolition company G. Tscherning. The waste composition, size, and project 
characterization can be seen in Table 15 below. The external processes used for the individual roof 
demolition modeling in EASETECH have been described in the following subsections.

Façade demolition F-4 
Project F-4 is the only demolished project which solely includes the façade demolition. It is a small 
demolition of 86.4 m2 for the removal of concrete sandwich elements to prepare for the attachment of 
an additional building. Sandwich elements are insulated according to current requirements for the en-
ergy framework. They consist of a front plate of 70 mm insulation in the standard thicknesses (50, 75, 
125, 150, 200, 225, 250, 275, or 300 mm) and a back plate in a minimum of 90 mm given that it was 
non-load-bearing walls. Furthermore, there are stainless steel binders and hanging brackets that hold 
the front plate to the back plate. None of this is separated but demolished in whole elements and trans-
ported to RGS for further sorting and recycling. Due to the concrete mix, a landfilling process for inert 
waste has been included after the waste reaches the recycling facility. Furthermore, the concrete is 
crushed to gravel, rather than new concrete due to its strength class.  
The full project included interior demolition, removal of wooden floors, kitchen floors, and beams as 
well as interior wall and floor cutting of a building from 2014. The entire project spanned from the 23rd 
of February to the 22nd of November 2019, but the façade demolition only had waste transportation 
from the 22nd of August to the 22nd of November 2019, and the total working days is set to 92 days 
==PAGE:51==
34  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
(weekends are included). The total person-hours of work used for this demolition was 142 hours. The 
modeled processes can be seen in Figure 6 and Table 20.

Façade and roof demolition RF-1 
The façade and roof demolition of project 180265 was done in four stages. Fiber cement cladding with 
bitumen on the roof was removed together with wood battens for a total area of 517 m2 for the first 
stage, 73 m2 for the third stage, and 478 m2 for the fourth and final stage. A total of 21 wood twigs and 
battens also had to be removed. Furthermore, nine skylights were dismantled and sorted together with 
windshield flashings, soil pipe flashings, and gutters with a total length of 171m as well as 400 mm 
insulation granules of 25 m2.  
The building is from 1964 and the full project spanned from the 4th of June 2018 to the 25th of April 
2021, resulting in total working days amounting to 9690 days (weekends are included). The total per-
son-hours of work used for this demolition was 51744 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in 
Figure 7 and Table 21.

Façade and roof demolition RF-2 
The combined roof and façade demolition called RF-2 is a demolition consisting of the dismantling of 
a 40 m2 hot roof construction (vapor barrier, tread mineral insulation, and roofing felt) as well as dem-
olition of 336 m2 cavity wall facing brick and insulation, wall binders, light expanded clay plinth stones, 
wall cardboard of polyester filt, and façade panels in a curved corner. The console beams of concrete, 
wall crown flashings of 131.6 m2 as well as wall insulation in the basement were also removed.  
The building is from 2016 and the project started on the 18th of February to the 2nd of December 2021 
which amount to 287 days (weekends are included). The total person-hours of work used for this dem-
olition was 735 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 8 and Table 22. 
 
Figure 8 Overview of the modeled combination of the roof and facade demolition project RF-2 in EASETECH.

Façade and roof demolition RF-3 
The last façade and roof demolition RF-3 is the newest of the projects which finished within the 
timeframe of this study. It, therefore, represents the newest demolition technology in Denmark. The 
demolition consists of the removal of roofing and roofing felt, and concrete wall crown with zinc clad-
ding. Furthermore, the project includes the disassembly and disposal of bearing, fastenings, etc. as well 
as the dismantling of foundations where all cutting in concrete must take place without water. 
The project started on the 5th of November 2021 to the 20th of May 2022 which amount to 196 days 
(weekends are included). The building was found to be constructed in 1994. The total person-hours of 
work used for this demolition was 974.5 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 9 and 
Table 23. 
 
Figure 9 Overview of the modeled combination of the roof and facade demolition project RF-3 in EASETECH.

4.3 Interior soft strip demolition 
For the LCA modeling of an interior soft strip demolition, data have been provided for six projects (SS-
1 the second smallest soft strip demolition, SS-2 the largest soft strip demolition, SS-3 which consists 
of primarily shredder, SS-4 which is the soft strip demolition with the second largest shredder fraction, 
SS-5 is the soft strip demolition with the largest waste variation, and SS-6 the smallest soft strip demo-
lition with only one combustible fraction) from the demolition company G. Tscherning. An interior 
strip can vary much in type and size, and therefore the projects have been described more in detail in 
the following sections. The external processes used for the modeling in EASETECH have also been 
described in the following subsections. The respective waste fractions for each project can be seen in 
Table 24.

Interior soft strip demolition SS-1 
Project SS-1 is the removal of specific walls, ceilings, and floors with a total area of 199 m2. Further-
more, the strip contains the removal of shelves and other stuff which hangs on the walls. The demolition 
was for a building constructed in 1991 and renovated again in 1997. The project started on December 
the 5th 2019 and lasted till January the 1st 2020 amounting to 27 days (weekends are included). The total 
person-hours of work used for this demolition was 142.5 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in 
Figure 10 and Table 25.

Interior soft strip demolition SS-2 
The scope of project SS-2 was the removal of all parts of the building's second floor, except electrical 
installations and plumber installations. Therefore, the interior wall, high-seated windowpanes, doors, 
foot panels, suspended ceilings, and floors (coatings, carpets, and linoleum, as well as chipboard sub-
floors and putty) are removed. In addition to this, the demolition included the removal of clinker and 
wall tiles as well as a small kitchenette. The total area of the floor is found to be 997 m2. Furthermore, 
the strip contains the removal of shelves and other stuff hanging on the walls.  
The building has been constructed in 1906 and renovated again in 1986. The project spanned from the 
20th of April to the 18th of June 2020 and thus lasted 59 days (weekends are included). The total person-
hours of work used for this demolition was 705 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 11 
and Table 26. 
 
Figure 11 Overview of modeled interior soft strip demolition project SS-2 in EASETECH.

Interior soft strip demolition SS-3 
Project SS-3 included the removal of 92 m2 walls, 315 m2 steel cassette ceilings, 2 pc. WC and sinks, 
as well as approximately 400 m2 ventilation system. The floor area is found to be 397.8 m2, but the 
chosen area for the analysis is given to 407 m2. The building was built in 2011 and the removal took 4 
days with waste transport only on the 30th of June 2020. The total person-hours of work used for this 
demolition was 225.5 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 12 and Table 27.

Interior soft strip demolition SS-4 
Project SS-2 was a simple demolition of interior plaster- and wooden walls, as well as the removal of a 
toilet on business premises on the ground floor. The total area of the floor is found to be 926 m2 in a 
building from 1928 (renovated in 1940). Furthermore, the strip contains the removal of the bicycle shed. 
The project lasted 5 days from the 21st till the 25th of September 2020 with 102.5 person-hours of work 
used. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 13 and Table 28.

Interior soft strip demolition SS-5 
This project was done in the same building from 1906 as project SS-2, but this project includes the 
waste from the third floor. The total area of the floor is the same as for R-3 of 997 m2. This project 
spanned from the 1st of July to the 18th of August 2021 and therefore this project lasted 49 days (week-
ends are included), which is 10 days faster than for the 2nd floor. The total person-hours of work used 
for this demolition was 826.5 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 14 and Table 29.

Interior soft strip demolition SS-6 
Project SS-6 is the removal of ventilation ducts as well as a simple interior clearing (not further de-
scribed). The ducts are not polluted and therefore the project cannot be included in the category of 
decontamination. It has been difficult to determine the area of the demolition. The total floor area has 
been provided from IOS (Udviklings- og forenklingsstyrelsen, 2022) and found to be 1800 m2 which 
could be overestimated. The building is from 1989. The project spanned from the 31st of November 
2020 to the 17th of January 2021 which is the same as 61 days with 102 person-hours of work used. The 
modeled processes can be seen in Figure 15 and Table 30.

4.4 Interior hard strip demolition 
For the LCA modeling of a hard strip demolition, data have been provided for six projects (HS-1 with 
the largest hard strip demolition and with the largest waste variation, HS-2 which was the average hard 
strip demolition, HS-3 with the removal of asbestos and hazardous waste, HS-4 the smallest hard strip 
demolition, HS-5 with the largest fraction of shredder and smallest waste variation) from the demolition 
company G. Tscherning. These projects are similar to a complete demolition; however, the structural 
element in the house remains, and, in this way, there will be less treatment of concrete and other heavy 
fractions. The projects are of different orders of magnitude and with varying waste compositions, which 
can be seen in Table 31 below. The external processes used for the modeling in EASETECH have been 
described in the following subsections.  
Table 31 The waste composition and project specifications for each of the FUs are divided into the demolition of six hard

Hard strip demolition HS-1 
Project 181097 is the largest hard strip demolition consisting of a total area of 19100 m2. The demolition 
included the removal of facades, floors, ceilings, walls, and installations. The roof was the only thing 
remaining on the building. Furthermore, the project included the use of a machine with 3194 l of diesel, 
14,674 m2 of sandblasting, as well as 1 m3 of water in 9.5 hours for sucking and washing down part of 
the building. After consulting the company, KM Afrensning, that the sandblasting utilizes 0.34 l/m2 A 
(KM Afrensning, n.d.). The suction and wash-down are modeled as water while it was found that the 
practice utilizes 4.7 l/hour (Norva24 and Sørensen, 2021).  
==PAGE:67==
50  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
This project spanned from the 4th of June 2018 to the 25th of April 2021 which amount to 1055 days 
(weekends are included). The total person-hours of work used for this demolition was 51,744 hours. 
The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 16 and Table 32.

Hard strip demolition HS-2 
Project HS-2 is an average hard strip demolition. The demolition work included the demolition of radi-
ators and pipes, demolition of all interior fittings, removal of floor coverings and tiles, removal of win-
dows, as well as cleaning of painted walls and ceilings. The building had to appear completely stripped 
before the renovation was conducted. The total area of the building was found to be 4225 m2. Further-
more, this project lasted from the 22nd of January 2019 to the 14th of December 2020. The total working 
period is thus 691 days (weekends are included). The total person-hours of work used for this demolition 
was 5198 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 17 and Table 33.

Hard strip demolition HS-3 
Project HS-3 is the same type of demolition as HS-1. This project utilized machine power, and thus the 
model includes the combustion of 5217 l of diesel for an excavator. This demolition is for an office 
building with a total area of 14500 m2. This project spanned 433 days (weekends are included) from the 
3rd of June 2019 to the 10th of August 2020. The total person-hours of work used for this demolition 
was 13,697.5 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 18 and Table 34.

4.5 Complete demolition 
For the LCA modeling of complete demolition, data have been provided for three projects (R-1 was the 
smallest complete demolition, R-2 included crushing of heavy fractions on site, and R-3 is the average 
complete demolition) from the demolition company G. Tscherning. The projects are of different orders 
of magnitude and with varying waste compositions, which can be seen in Table 15. The external pro-
cesses used for the modeling in EASETECH have been described in the following subsections. Further-
more, the freight lorry used for the heavy fractions is set to be 16-32 tons rather than 7.5-16 tons from 
the project site to the recycling facility. This is the best representative of the use of a semi-trailer used 
in projects that entail the removal of enormous amounts of concrete.  
Table 37 The waste composition and project specifications for each of the FUs are divided into the complete demolitions.

Complete demolition CD-1 
Project CD-1 is the smallest of the complete demolitions of one postal service building with an unknown 
construction year (set to 1910 as most of the postal service buildings were built between 1900 and 
1921). The total area of the building, including the basement, is found to be 2102 m2. A complete dem-
olition is the complete removal of a building, and therefore includes the removal of the roof, walls, 
building foot, basement, etc. For all demolition practices in Denmark, the materials are removed with 
the machine which generates the least noise and dust. In the case of dusty works, the dust is controlled 
at the source, and decontamination boxes with coarse filters are used to extract air. When removing 
contaminated materials and sandblasting, decontamination boxes and/or vacuum cleaners with fine fil-
ters are utilized, in accordance with current regulations. 
==PAGE:79==
62  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
For the complete demolition of constructions, the waste constituted of heavy fractions: Concrete, light-
weight concrete, and roof tiles, as well as light fractions: roofing felt, gypsum, insulation, etc. The 
demolition was conducted with both machines and by hand. This demolition also included sandblasting 
of 1271 m2, suction and wash down of 0.31 t waste with 0.5 m3 water by subcontractors, modeled in 
the same way as project HS-1.  
The project started on the 4th of April 2018 to the 1st of January 2020 which amount to 636 days (week-
ends are included). The total person-hours of work used for this demolition was 2583.5 hours. The 
modeled processes can be seen in Figure 21 and Table 38.

Complete demolition CD-2 
Project CD-2 follows the same structure as the previously described demolition. The contract included 
decontamination, demolition, and disposal of four buildings in Copenhagen. The total area of the build-
ings, including basements, is found to be 3930 m2. Here, one of the buildings must be split in two and 
be fully functional during the entire demolition period. Between the current building part which re-
mained and the part of the building that was demolished, a separating wall was built so that part of the 
building and the basement could be cut off from the demolition. This presented additional obstacles in 
the form of care in the demolition practice. This was considered early in the design phase of the demo-
lition practice such that the cost of securing this wall from damage during free cutting and demolition 
was included. In general, the demolition contractor had to cut through the existing basement, both in 
the base plate, walls, and "ceiling/roof" with a diamond cutter. The diamond cutting has not been mod-
eled, as it was not possible to find a similar process in either EASETECH or ecoinvent. This is assumed 
to be of minor importance as the impact primarily would be from carbon emissions in the use phase and 
is thus expected to have less importance by the repeated use of the tools (Li et al., 2017). 
This demolition also included sandblasting of 51844 m2 and suction and wash down of 3.42 t waste 
with 3 m3 water by subcontractors, modeled in the same way as project HS-1. In addition to this the 
concrete and other heavy fractions, in total 3123 t were crushed on site and used as aggregates and soil 
stabilization. This process requires 0.5 l diesel/ton crushed heavy fraction material (MIS Recycling, 
2020). Given that 60% of 76% of heavy fractions remained on site, the vehicle is set to a 7.5-16-ton 
EURO5 lorry truck from the site to the recycling facility. For the modeling, the waste amount is only 
done for the waste leaving the site. The project started on the 4th of June 2019 to the 21st of January 
2020 which amount to 231 days (weekends are included). The total person-hours of work used for this 
demolition was 5809 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 22 and Table 39. 
 
Figure 22 Overview of the modeled complete demolition project CD-2 in EASETECH.

Complete demolition CD-3 
Project CD-3 follows the same structure as the CD-1. The contract included decontamination, demoli-
tion, and disposal of two buildings in Copenhagen. The total area of the building, including the base-
ment, is found to be 3980 m2 with 2100 m2 of pavement. This demolition also included sandblasting of 
7328 m2 modeled in the same way as previously described. The project started on the 14th of January to 
the 22nd of May 2020 which amount to 119 days (weekends are included). The total person-hours of 
work used for this demolition was 3959,5 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 23 and 
Table 40.

4.6 Decontamination 
For the LCA modeling of decontamination of buildings, data have been provided for five projects (DC-
1 was the largest decontamination project, DC-2 was the average decontamination project, DC-3 where 
only plaster waste was removed, DC-4 was the removal of asbestos insulated pipes, and DC-5 which 
was the removal of asbestos insulated pipe bends) from the demolition company G. Tscherning. These 
projects are smaller in size compared to other projects, and some only contain one waste fraction. For 
projects DC-4 and DC-5, it was not possible to estimate an area, so instead a distance of decontamina-
tion has been used, which can be seen in Table 41. The external processes used for the modeling in 
EASETECH have been described in the following subsections. Due to the lesser amounts of waste, a 
small EURO5 lorry truck is used for 3.5-7.5 tons.

Decontamination DC-1 
Project DC-1 is decontamination from PCB on an entire floor area of a 5-story tall building. The de-
contamination included windows, windows ledge, and vacuuming. PCB was found in the weld joint of 
the window, but as each floor consists of 219 windows an exchange of these would be too expensive. 
Therefore, the window ledge and 0.5 m of the wall in each room (PCB travels from a contaminated 
ledge to the concrete surrounding it as well as evaporates into the room (Realdania, 2021)) were decon-
taminated with PCB as well. The decontaminated floor area is found to be 23 m2. 
The building was from 1971. The project started on the 3rd of April 2019 to the 29th of January 2020 
which amount to 301 days (weekends are included). The total person-hours of work used for this dem-
olition was 1199 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 24 and Table 42.

Decontamination DC-2 
Project DC-2 is the decontamination of a floor area of 85.8 m2 with asbestos. The scrapping of the 
casted floor was a maximum of 30 mm. The building was built in 1895 and had previously been used 
as a pipe factory. Furthermore, some of the surfaces of the cold outer wall had mold and thus additional 
waste fractions include wood and mixed waste. The project spanned 9 days (weekends are included) 
from the 10th to the 18th of December 2019. The total person-hours of work used for this demolition was 
69.5 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Table 43. It was decided to exclude the material 
reprocessing of wood and insulation from the waste composition as the waste amount was so small.

Decontamination DC-3 
Project DC-3 is also a decontamination of a floor area (2224 m2) in a building from 1957. Here black 
molding found on the ground floor contains asbestos, as well as low concentrations of PCBs, PAHs, 
and heavy metals. Due to a large oil concentration, the floor had to be classified as hazardous waste. In 
Fortum, it is not allowed to accept materials containing asbestos, and thus it was sent for landfilling in 
the mixed waste category. Furthermore, the lighter floor area was found to contain several boring chem-
ical substances which are used, for example, in adhesives under PVC floors, in wood products, etc. Oil 
substances have also been found (including the lighter ones that can cause indoor climate problems), 
and thus the waste had to be classified as contaminated and sent to a landfill.  
The project started on the 16th of April to the 29th of May 2020 which amount to 43 days (weekends are 
included). The total person-hours of work used for this demolition was 209.5 hours. The project also 
included 8 hours of suction and wash down, modeled as a diesel consumption of 4.7 l/hour (Norva24 
and Sørensen, 2021). The total water consumption is also found to be 1000 l. The modeled processes 
can be seen in Figure 26 and Table 44.

Decontamination DC-4 
Project DC-4 is an asbestos decontamination of the insulation surrounding a 100 m pipe system. The 
floor area has not been found, but instead, the length is defined as 100 m as explained in Table 41.  
The building was from 1907 but had been renovated in 1980. The renovation did not impact the asbestos 
around the piping system as the utilization of asbestos became illegal in 1972 in Denmark (Arbejdstil-
synet, 2019). The project only took two days between the 17th and 18th of March 2021. The total person-
hours of work used for this demolition was 45 hours. The modeled processes can be seen in Figure 27.

5.0 Life cycle impact assessment (LCIA) results  
LCIA results from the 15 environmental impact categories (both characterized and normalized) have 
been calculated for a total of the 26 demolition projects on office buildings. The full overview of mid-
point and endpoint results for each model can be seen in the Excel Appendix EASETECH results sheet 
environmental impacts. This section will provide results based on each demolition subcategory and 
compare each demolition model. Following the results are a contribution analysis and a sensitivity anal-
ysis. The interpretation of the LCIA results is presented following an uncertainty analysis. The inter-
pretation will include references to the existing literature as well as provide a broader perspective of the 
study.  
For each model, the environmental impact has been calculated for all FU, as mentioned in section 3.3.3 
LCI modeling framework and handling of multifunctional processes, however, it has been chosen to 
primarily focus on CC and RD-m.  
5.1 LCIA results for the roof demolition  
The characterized and normalized LCIA results on CC and RD-m have been found for each roof model 
per total demolished area. The results for each of the remaining impact categories can be found in the 
Excel Appendix EASETECH results sheet R-Samlet.  
Table 47 Characterized and normalized LCIA results for each roof demolition.

The results on CC and RD-m can be read in Table 47, where the emissions of kg CO2-eq/m2 per project 
have a smaller variation in the results as the standard deviation is closer to zero compared to the results 
of the other demolition subcategories. There is no trend between the total waste amount per square 
meter and the emission of kg CO2-eq.  
Project R-3 which included the removal of an aluminum roof contributed to less emission of CO2-eq. 
Furthermore, project R-2 illustrates that the removal of the slate eternity roof contributes to the largest 
emission of CO2-eq. Project R-1 is a result of how the refurbishment of a roof consisting of tiles would 
emit CO2-eq to the atmosphere.  
For the normalized values, the roof demolition impact on CC is a factor 1000 greater than RD-m. For 
the results on RD-m, the variation is even closer to zero compared to the CC, which is partly related to 
the values being closer to zero. Furthermore, the two roof demolitions contribute to a saving in the 
environmental effect. The largest saving is calculated for the roof demolition with aluminum plates, 
which relates well to the smaller impact on CC. For the removal of slate eternity project R-2, where no 
materials are sent to material reprocessing and substitution, the demolition and waste treatment process 
in itself only contributes to a small degree of burden for the RD-m. Had the production of the materials 
been within the system boundary it is expected that the impact burden on RD-m would be larger.  
Lastly, the impact also follows the construction year for the three roof demolitions. This means that the 
older the construction is the larger the impact burden in both impact categories. Both projects R-1 and 
R-3 have roof materials that have been used for approximately 55 years, while the asbestos roof for 
project R-2 has been in use for more than 115 years. Slate eternity is a construction material built with 
==PAGE:94==
77  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
a long-lasting lifetime, and thus the overall impact could have been smaller if the production of mate-
rials had been incorporated into the calculations of the LCIA. Also, the impact from the demolition of 
projects R-1 and R-2 had to be doubled if the lifetime of roof R-1 had been used.  
5.1.1 Contribution analysis for the roof demolition 
A contribution analysis has been made for the roof demolition project. Here, the impacts have been 
aggregated into 7 life cycle stages: Recycling, Incineration, and special treatment incineration (C3), 
Landfill (C4), transport from a demolition site to the recycling facility and final disposal (C3), demoli-
tion (C1), and material reprocessing (D). The contribution from each process to the impact categories 
of CC and RD-m has been illustrated in Figure 29, while the remaining contribution analysis in each 
impact category can be seen in the Excel Appendix EASETECH results sheet Contribution.  
 
Figure 29 Contribution analysis for the roof demolition in the impact categories of climate change and mineral resource 
scarcity. 
Figure 29 illustrates that for CC the demolition activity is the process that contributes the greatest, while 
for RD-m the largest contribution is found for the material reprocessing and substitution. The material 
substitution process contributes almost similar to the aluminum and roof tile demolition. The waste 
incineration also contributes to environmental savings for both the CC and the RD-m impact category. 
Hazardous waste, landfill, recycling, and transportation are all processes that contribute slightly to the 
environmental effect in both impact categories.

the demolition process, see Table 48, the largest contribution to the CC impact category is the consump-
tion of electricity for the site accommodation. For the roof projects involving the removal of an alumi-
num plate and roof tiles, the electricity consumption was calculated to be contributing 95.5% and 88.1% 
of the total demolition process, respectively. For project R-2 which was the removal of slate eternity 
the electricity consumption contributed to 81.1% while the contribution from employee commuting was 
2.4 times larger than that of transport for materials. For the aluminum roof demolition, the transport of 
materials contributed 40% of the amount of the employee combined and material transport combined. 
Contrary to this trend, for the regular roof tile removal demolition, the material transport was 6 times 
larger than the employee commuting. This could be related to the fact that this project had a project 
period that was approximately 4 times longer and the total waste amount was an average of 16 times 
larger compared to that of the other analyzed roof projects. The additional transport of materials could 
therefore be related to lack of space availability, the warehouse being more theft proofed, or the renting 
period being costlier than the transport back and forth.  
In Table 48 it is also found that the reprocessing of shredder and substitution of aluminum is the largest 
contributor to a saving in the RD-m. This could be related to the unit for the characterized value of RD-
m given in Cu-eq. There is expected to be more copper retrieval from the shredder fraction than any 
other construction material reprocessed from a construction site.  
5.1.2 Cost estimate for roof demolition 
Following the contribution analysis, Table 49 illustrates the cost of the processes within the demolition 
activity, the various material treatment options, and the transportation. Here the demolition activity 
alone is the costliest of the three overall processes. The largest cost within the demolition activity is 
found for the employees, which is also the least environmentally harmful “process” compared to fuel 
consumption, transport, and machines for subcontractors. Within the demolition process, the electricity 
consumption has not been priced. This process is an expense of the contractor and therefore the lineup 
and maintenance of electricity and water consumption for the site accommodation is not part of the 
demolition contract.
