For the waste treatment option, the recycling treatment is the most expensive, which relates to the largest 
waste fraction being sent there. For the roof project revolving around the removal of slate eternity, the 
landfilling process has the largest cost of the waste treatment options. To better compare the cost of the 
waste treatment options, the price per kg of waste managed was found. Here recycling costs approx. 
500 DKK/ton waste, landfill cost approx. 2100 DKK/ton waste, and special treatment cost approx. 5600 
DKK/ton waste. In this way, recycling becomes the cheapest waste treatment option. As found in the 
contribution analysis the recycling process does not have a significant contribution compared to the 
==PAGE:96==
79  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
remaining waste categories. The special treatment and landfilling of waste have the largest cost per 
amount of waste.  
Despite waste being sent to waste incineration, the cost cannot be applied in the above cost estimate as 
the combustible waste for these projects is sent to a recycling facility first. This is a delimitation of the 
cost estimate of the study. The reason for this can be to avoid the additional cost for person-hours of 
work as it is required to downsize and divide the combustible elements to fit the incinerator opening. 
The cost of person-hours of work is more costly than sending combustibles to a recycling facility.  
The transport process has a higher cost compared to its overall contribution. This is because it was not 
possible to separate the cost of transporting materials and transporting waste. Overall, the removal of 
slate eternity roofing is the most expensive form of roof demolition while it is also the most environ-
mentally harmful roof demolition in the CC and RD-m impact category. Related to the construction 
year, R-2 with slate eternity is the most expensive which is more related to the construction materials 
than the actual lifetime of the building. The projects R-1 (from 1964) and R-2 (from 1970) cost approx-
imately the same.  
 
5.2 LCIA results for the combined roof and façade demolition  
The characterized and normalized LCIA results on CC and RD-m have been calculated for each com-
bined roof and façade model per total demolished area. The results for each of the remaining impact 
categories can be found in the Excel Appendix EASETECH results sheet RF-Samlet. The LCIA results 
on CC and RD-m for the combined roof and façade demolition can be seen in Table 50. As found for 
the LCIA results of the roof demolition, the waste amount does not influence the CC impact.  
The largest impact is found for the demolitions which consist primarily of heavy fractions such as a 
concrete wall (RF-3) or a cavity wall facing brick (RF-2). The larger variation in the results compared 
to the roof demolition is supported by the standard deviation being further from zero in both impact 
categories. This variation is primarily due to project RF-1 which results in environmental savings in 
both impact categories given that the shredder was reprocessed and substituted and a larger fraction of 
waste sent to the incineration was found.

compared to that of the roof demolition as the impact on CC is a factor of 100 greater than RD-m.

==PAGE:97==

the combined roof and façade demolitions with a significant amount of heavy waste fractions. This 
seems plausible since project F-4 only consists of the removal of concrete. Here the impact burden from 
the single waste fraction project is much larger, which can be attributed to no incineration process con-
tributing to the environmental saving. Furthermore, the heavier fractions are less valuable material sub-
stitutes compared to metal. For the CC impact category, the impact from the façade project is much 
larger than the combined roof and façade demolitions.  
Contrary to the roof demolitions the oldest buildings have the largest burden on the CC impact category. 
Project RF-1 and project RF-2 are built between 2014 and 2016, respectively. With the same lifetime 
as building RF-3 from 1994, the production and demolition of R-1 and R-2 would have to be added to 
itself 4 and 6 times. For project R-1 this seems good from the demolition perspective, however, this 
also includes the production of steel materials which would contribute to a larger burden.  
5.2.1 Contribution analysis for the façade and combined roof and facade demolition 
As for the roof demolition, a contribution analysis has also been made for the façade and the combined 
roof and façade demolition projects. The contribution from each process on the impact categories of 
CC and RD-m has been illustrated in Figure 30, while the remaining contribution analysis on the re-
maining impact category can be seen in the Excel Appendix EASETECH results sheet Contribution.  
 
Figure 30 Contribution analysis for the façade and façade/roof demolition in the impact categories climate change and min-
eral resource scarcity. 
Figure 30 visualize that the demolition process again contributes to the largest burden in the CC impact 
categories, except for project RF-1 where the material reprocessing and substitution contribute to the 
greatest impact. The demolition process also contributes to the largest burden in the RD-m impact cat-
egory. Contrary, in Figure 29 it was seen that for RD-m the largest contribution was calculated for the 
material reprocessing and substitution. For the façade and combined roof and façade demolition, this is 
only the case for project RF-1 which had the smallest fraction of heavy fractions as well as being the 
only project with a fraction of shredder. For the façade demolition only consisting of concrete, the 
material reprocessing only contributes to a small part (-20%) for the RD-m and not at all (-0.5%) for 
the CC impact. This aligns well with the contribution variation of the other combined roof and façade 
demolition with a greater amount of heavy waste fractions.  
The waste incineration only contributes to a middling part (20%) of the environmental saving for CC 
for project RF-1. Furthermore, recycling contributes to a larger part of the CC impact for projects with 
a larger share of heavy fractions. It is expected that the recycling facilities utilize more fuel at the recy-
cling facility with the crushing of concrete and other heavy fractions, and thus this correlates well with 
the contribution of recycling being larger for the projects with a greater share of heavy fractions.

For the waste treatment option, the recycling treatment is the most expensive, which, similar to the roof 
demolition, relates to the largest waste fraction being sent there. Also, recycling is the cheapest waste 
treatment process per kg of waste. The price per kg of waste increases when waste is sent to a landfill

to its overall contribution.

than the recycling. Given that waste incineration and recycling has approximately the same contribution 
it correlated well with the cost. There is no trend between the construction year of the building materials 
and the cost of the demolition.  
 
5.3 LCIA results for the soft trip demolition  
The characterized and normalized LCIA results on CC and RD-m have been calculated for each soft 
strip demolition model per total demolished area. The results for each of the remaining impact catego-
ries can be found in the Excel Appendix EASETECH results sheet SS-Samlet.  
The results on CC and RD-m can be read in Table 53. Compared to the roof and façade demolitions the 
variation for the soft strip demolition lies between that of the roof and façade demolition in both impact 
categories. This is related to project SS-3 which has a large impact saving for the CC impact, while the 
remaining projects lie between -10 to 10. The projects with an impact burden have no immediate outliers 
such as project F-4 for the façade demolition. Overall, the CC impact from the soft strip is smaller 
compared to the roof and façade demolition, which could be related to a smaller waste amount and 
fraction variety. Similar to the roof and façade demolition there is no trend between the total waste 
amount per square meter and the emission of kg CO2-eq for each project.  
The projects with the smallest waste amount (SS-1, SS-5, and SS-6) contribute to a burden on the CC 
impact category. The larger saving on the CC impact category from projects SS-3 and SS-4 could be 
related to the larger fraction of shredder in the overall waste composition.

5.3.1 Contribution analysis for the soft strip demolition 
A contribution analysis has also been made for the soft strip demolition projects. The contribution from 
each process to the impact categories of CC and RD-m has been illustrated in Figure 31, while the 
remaining contribution analysis in each impact category can be seen in the Excel Appendix EASETECH 
results sheet Contribution. Figure 31 illustrates more variation in the contributing processes compared 
to the roof and façade demolition subcategory. In the CC impact category, the demolition or incineration 
process contributes the most. For projects SS-4 and SS-3, the material reprocessing and substitution 
also contribute to a larger saving in the CC impact. Looking into each of the individual processes for 
the CC impact category the electricity contributes the most to the demolition process, also as concluded 
for the roof and façade demolitions. Except for project SS-4, here the demolition process only involves 
one material transport and neither car transport nor electricity consumption. For project SS-1 and project 
SS-2 the employee commuting contributes more than the transport of equipment. For projects SS-3 and 
SS-5, there is no difference in the contribution of material transport and car transport within the demo-
lition category. Project SS-6 was the only project where the transport of equipment and materials con-
tributed to a larger part than the employee commuting. Despite more projects having a larger contribu-
tion for the car transport compared to the material transport which is similar to the conclusion from the 
roof, it has not been possible to determine a significant variation for these two categories within the 
demolition process activity.  
 
Figure 31 Contribution analysis for the soft strip demolition in the impact categories of climate change and mineral re-
source scarcity. 
For projects SS-3 and project SS-4, the reprocessing and material substitution of steel contribute to the 
largest CC impact saving. Within the steel substitution process, the avoidance of burning fossil fuels 
and methane for the virgin production of steel is what contributes to the largest saving. Per kg of metal 
scrap which is being reprocessed the impact is 0.12 kg CO2-eq which seems like a minimal impact 
reprocessing process compared to the production of new steel materials.  
For the RD-m impact category, the demolition process contributes to half of the impact as a burden for 
projects SS-1 and SS-2. For project SS-6 the demolition process contributes to 87% of the entire impact, 
also as a burden. For the three projects (SS-3, SS-4, and SS-5) for which the shredder waste fraction is 
part of the waste composition, the material reprocessing and substitution have the largest impact. There 
is almost no difference in how much the material contribution affects the RD-m despite the variation of 
metal in the waste composition of each waste category.

the substance specific emissions from the waste-to-energy plant itself. For the waste-to-energy-plant 
process, it is found that the electricity substitution following the heat substitution contributes to the

The waste incineration plant itself has also been analyzed for its substance specific contributions. Here 
it is the burning of carbon contained products such as wood and other combustibles, which contribute 
to the largest burden for the CC impact category. It is also the removal of uranium, iron, and nickel 
from the incineration cleaning process which contributes to the environmental saving in the RD-m im-
pact category. The saving is related to the avoided use of nuclear power plants as uranium is an infre-
quent substance that is very rarely found in the construction materials for office buildings in Denmark. 
This saving contributes to uncertainty for the RD-m impact category. The removal of iron and Nickel 
could be related to bad sorting and the mixed waste categories. Nickel is included in construction waste 
on painted surfaces. 
The similar percentages are caused by the crediting of energy which in ecoinvent would be the Swedish 
nuclear power plant. When the credited energy is normalized the percentage outcome is the same given 
that the same process is the most sensitive. It is therefore necessary to look at the overall characterized 
value for the WtE to identify which project has the largest saving. For the soft strip demolition project, 
this is seen for SS-2 which is the project with the largest waste fraction sent to incineration. More 
decimals could provide more difference in the numbers; however, this also introduces more uncertainty 
and since it is more relevant to investigate the overall saving percentages from the WtE process it was 
deemed irrelevant for this study.

demolition activity alone is the costliest of the three overall processes. The trend with the largest cost 
for labor is also followed. There is no cost to the landfill or special treatment.

olition (StD = 13.8 and 0.41) in both impact categories as these demolition subcategories have a larger 
variety in the waste composition for each project.

Similar to the previously analyzed demolition subcategories, there is no trend between the total waste 
amount per square meter and the emission of kg CO2-eq per square meter.  
Projects HS-1 and HS-3 had the largest emissions of CO2-eq which could be related to the total amount 
of waste generated or because these projects were the only ones also involving the removal of asbestos 
and hazardous waste. Project HS-1 was also the project with the most variation in the waste composi-
tion. Project HS-4 is the hard strip demolition with the lowest emissions of CO2-eq, which could be 
related to the project constituting the most waste per square area while project HS-5 is also the project 
conducted in the shortest timeframe. Project HS-5 had a saving in CO2-eq which could be related to the 
project being conducted without the use of machines and with the least variety of waste fractions. The 
result from the RD-m also illustrates that much of the waste contributed to a saving in the impact, which, 
as previously discussed, is related to the substitution of materials. Since similar fractions are sent to a 
waste handler from a hard strip demolition, the effect on CC is limited by the number of different waste 
==PAGE:103==
86  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
fractions and treatment methods. The difference in the composition included waste fractions such as 
asbestos, hazardous waste, mixed waste (category 1), soil, and wood.  
According to the construction of the building materials, a project built in the same construction era 
follow the impacts. Such that HS-1 and HS-3 from 1967 have approximately the same impact burden 
in both impact categories, while projects HS-4 and HS-5 from 1975 and 1974, respectively, both have 
environmental savings in both impact categories. Project HS-2 is from 1957 and here the impact is in 
between. From the façade demolition, that project F-4 from 1964 had the largest impact burden while 
the roof demolition project R-1 also had a larger impact compared to project R-3 from 1970. This could 
imply that the contaminated building elements used in that period contribute to a larger burden for the 
demolition and waste management impact later in the building’s life cycle.  
For all hard strip demolitions, an environmental saving can be spotted in the RD-m impact category. 
With more recyclable waste, the impact from demolition on the mineral resource category is larger 
(except for project HS-3 which also had both asbestos and hazardous waste). The largest saving is found 
for project HS-4 which was the hard strip demolition with the smallest area. The project which had the 
second largest area also had the smallest impact saving for the RD-m. As such, no significant average 
on value RD-m saving can be found across the impact per demolished area.  
For the normalized values, the trend for hard strip demolition is smaller than for the roof demolition 
with the impact on CCs being an average only a factor of 100 greater than that of RD-m.

den in the CC impact category, except for project HS-4 and project HS-5 where the reprocessing and 
substitution of materials contribute more. For project HS-1 the contribution is varied such that the waste 
incineration process contributes the most, however, this contribution is not as significant and only con-
tributes 5 % more than the demolition process. For RD-m the largest saving and contribution is again 
found for the material reprocessing and substitution across all projects.

previously with the recycling being the most expensive overall due to the largest fraction being sent 
there, but the cheapest per ton of waste. Lastly, the transport process is one of the less costly activities 
which assimilate its contribution to the overall effect in both categories.

Project HS-4 is the costliest of the projects per square meter in the hard strip demolition subcategory. 
This could be related to the fact that this demolition is also the smallest. The same processes are needed 
for the remaining projects, however with more limited space to practice the demolition. The cheapest 
demolition in the hard strip demolition subcategory is project HS-2 and project HS-3 where the area of 
project HS-3 is three times larger than that of project HS-2. Therefore, as the price is divided by the 
area the cost of the project becomes alike. Interestingly, project HS-3 contains asbestos and hazardous 
waste in its waste composition while project HS-2 does not. The projects differ from the remaining 
projects as these include a greater amount of concrete and heavy waste fractions than the remaining 
hard strip demolitions. These fractions are the cheapest to dispose of. The most environmentally friendly 
project in both impact categories is also found to be the most expensive project per square area. No 
trend can however be identified as project HS-5 is categorized as a cheap project but has the second 
largest environmental saving in both impact categories, while project HS-1 has the largest impact bur-
den for the CC impact category but is the second costliest project. There is no trend between the con-
striction year of the materials and the cost of the demolition.  
Compared to the roof, façade, and soft strip demolition the cost of a hard strip demolition is exactly 
similar to that of a soft strip demolition. The hard strip demolition was similar to the soft strip demolition 
also the least harmful of the demolition subcategories for CC. The effect from the demolitions with the 
heavy fractions also contributes more to the burden on CC (therefore not soft strip and hard strip dem-
olition with a fractional smaller percentage of heavier fractions).

5.5 LCIA results for complete demolition  
In Table 60, each complete demolition model per total demolished area has been calculated for the 
characterized and normalized LCIA results on CC and RD-m. The results for each of the remaining 
impact categories can be found in the excel Appendix EASETECH results sheet CD-Samlet. This is the 
demolition subcategory that has the greatest variation in the results of the demolition subcategory. This 
type of demolition also provides the most amount of waste which could contribute to the larger margin 
for the impact depending on the waste composition, and how the waste is separated into fractions and 
handled at the waste facilities. Furthermore, the complete demolition is the demolition activity that has 
the largest effect on CC.

There is also no trend between the total waste amount per square meter and the emission of kg CO2-eq 
per square meter. Project CD-1 is the complete demolition that composes the largest burden to both the 
CC and mineral resource impact category. This is the project with the least waste fractions. Project CD-
2 is the project with the most variation in the waste fractions, and thus also with the most waste fractions 
overall, and this is the project with the lowest impact on both impact categories. This is the opposite 
trend as found for the hard strip demolitions where the lowest impact was found for the project with the 
least different waste fractions and vice versa. Instead, it can be explained that project CD-1 had the 
overall smallest waste amount while project CD-2 had the overall largest waste amount for the complete 
demolition projects. Project CD-2 also crushed the heavy fractions on the demolition site which con-
tributed to a large saving in transport and recycling processes. Project CD-1 was the complete demoli-
tion project which took the longest time to conduct, which contradicts the results of CC savings found 
for project HS-1 for the hard strip demolitions.  
The oldest building had the largest impact (CD-1) and vice versa for the newest building (CD-2). On 
the contrary, most polluting substances can be found in the newest building from 1973. This show that 
good project planning is essential for a better environmental impact from a demolition.  
The result from the RD-m only illustrates a saving in resources for project CD-2, which is the only 
project where metal is transported directly away from the site. No significant average value for the RD-
m saving can be found across the impact per demolished area.  
For the normalized values, the trend for the complete demolitions follows that of the roof demolitions 
with the impact on CCs being a factor 10000 greater than RD-m. Project CD-2 is the only complete 
demolition project with the removal of asbestos, which in this case, compared to the effect from the 
remaining waste fraction, seems to have an insignificant effect on both CC and RD-m.  
5.5.1 Contribution analysis for the complete demolition 
A contribution analysis has been made for the complete demolition projects. The contribution from each 
process to the impact categories of CC and RD-m has been illustrated in Figure 33 below. The remaining 
contribution analysis in each impact category can be seen in the Excel Appendix EASETECH results 
sheet Contribution. For the complete demolitions, there is a larger variation in the contribution from the 
various activities. Here special treatment and recycling have a larger contribution, compared to the 
==PAGE:107==
90  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
previously analyzed demolition subcategories. Demolition still contributes to the largest burden in both 
the CC and the RD-m impact category, except for project CD-2 where material substitution contributes 
to a larger saving for the RD-m impact category. 
 
Figure 33 Contribution analysis for the complete demolitions in the impact categories of climate change and mineral re-
source scarcity. 
In Table 61, it is found that the largest contributor to CC within the demolition process is the electricity 
consumption, however, the contribution is smaller than for the remaining demolition processes within 
each demolition subcategory. The transport of equipment and materials contributes significantly more 
than for the remaining demolition subcategories, which again could be related to the warehouse being 
more theft proofed or the renting period being costlier than the transport back and forth. The excavator 
is also used significantly more for projects which need the removal of fundaments and heavier fractions. 
The crushing of heavy fractions and concrete on site contributes to a saving in CC while it also contrib-
utes to the largest saving for the RD-m impact category. Besides the saving from the recycling of con-
crete and heavy fractions on site, the electricity consumption for the site accommodation and the 
transport of materials with a truck contributes to the largest impact in the RD-m impact category. For 
project CD-2 where the heavy fractions are crushed on site, there is less transport of equipment and 
materials. For all complete demolition projects, the transport of materials contributes to a larger CC 
burden than that of employee commuting. This contradicts the findings for the hard strip demolition.

Within the material reprocessing category in Table 61, the reprocessing and substitution of shredder, 
insulation, and the crushing and substitution of gravel have the largest contributions to a saving in the 
CC impact category. When the shredder fraction is not part of the waste composition, the saving from 
insulation and gravel increase significantly. Furthermore, the impact from wood, concrete, and asphalt 
substitution does not have a significant effect. Again, this could be related to such a small amount being 
substituted as referred to in Table 14 and recycled for other than energy recovery for wood. Compost 
contributes to a negative impact on CC but has almost no impact on the RD-m impact category.  
In Figure 33, it is visualized that the incineration process varies for each of the complete demolition 
projects, which align with the other demolition subcategories, except for the hard strip. The waste in-
cineration also contributes to a small environmental saving for the RD-m. For project CD-2 with the 
most mixed waste fractions, the waste incineration contributes the smallest to the RD-m impact cate-
gory. The substance specific impact on the waste incineration plant illustrates the same values as for 
the soft strip demolition.  
The special treatment has also been analyzed as substance specific for the complete demolition as it has 
a larger impact on the two impact categories compared to the remaining demolition subcategories. Here 
the burning of fossil carbon dioxide contributes to the largest burden for the CC impact category. This 
is the same argument as for the general waste incineration. For the RD-m, the complete burning of iron, 
nickel, and copper contributes to the environmental burden on the RD-m impact. The elevated temper-
atures for a special treatment facility will convert usable substances into nothing, and remove all sub-
stances bound to material with the hazardous substances.  
5.5.2 Cost estimate for the complete demolition 
The cost of the demolition, waste treatment, and transport processes in the complete demolition activity 
has been portrayed in Table 62 below. The trend follows that of the previously analyzed demolition 
subcategories, where the cost of the employees is the largest cost in the demolition activity. Here the 
machines and subcontractors also have a costlier role to play compared to the roof, façade, soft- and 
hard-strip demolition. This is because more machines and subcontractors are utilized for the complete 
demolition projects. The treatment option also follows the same trend as seen previously with the recy-
cling being the most expensive overall due to the largest fraction being sent there, but the cheapest per 
ton of waste. Transport is one of the less costly activities which matches well with the fact that the 
transport process is one of the smallest contributors to the overall effect in both impact categories.

There is not a large variation in cost for each of the complete demolition projects. This could be related 
to the fact that the complete demolitions often have more space availability, as well as a more similar 
demolition description and activity whereas the other demolition subcategories are very dependent on 
the waste composition and project description or management. The costliest project is CD-2 where the 
heavy fractions are demolished on the ground. There is no trend between the cost of the demolition and 
the construction year of the materials and the building.  
Compared to the roof, façade, and soft strip demolition the cost for a complete demolition is the costli-
est. Compared to the hard strip the complete demolition is cheaper. It should be noted that the hard strip 
demolition varies much in the overall cost. A reason for the cost of the complete demolition being 
smaller than that of the hard strip could be related to the fact that heavier fractions are cheaper to recycle, 
while also the hard strip demolition is more limited in space compared to the complete demolition. 
Complete demolition was the most harmful of the demolition subcategory for CC per area. This could 
be related to the heavy fractions, which it has been found previously, to contribute more to the burden 
on CC.  
 
5.6 LCIA results for decontamination  
LCIA results have been provided for each of the decontamination projects per total demolished area. 
Table 63 illustrates the characterized and normalized LCIA results on CC and RD-m. The results for 
each of the remaining impact categories can be found in the Excel Appendix EASETECH results sheet 
DC-Samlet. Despite the decontamination projects being the smallest type of demolition, the impact on 
CC is similar to that of the roof demolition. This is the only demolition subcategory where an average 
burden was calculated for the RD-m impact category as this is a demolition process that consists of the 
removal of non-recyclable hazardous substances. The variation in results is similar to that of the soft 
strip demolition. This could be related to the fact that the type of demolition activity varies for each 
project as well as the size and material composition.

There is no trend between the total waste amount per square meter and the emission of kg CO2-eq per 
square meter. The projects DC-2, DC-3, and DC-5 had the largest burden on CC. The projects are vastly 
different as project DC-2 constitute of three different waste fraction including asbestos, project DC-3 
only include plaster waste from concrete, and projects DC-4 and DC-5 only constitute of asbestos. The 
area for projects DC-4 and DC-5 is given in meters and therefore cannot be compared one to one with 
the other decontamination projects. Their results are very dissimilar, and a trend cannot be obtained for 
the removal of asbestos. Project DC-1 had the largest area of decontamination, almost three times as 
large as the area of project DC-3 which could contribute to the lower impact on both CC and RD-m. 
Project DC-1 and project DC-2 both had three different waste fractions, but the result from these pro-
jects is not similar and cannot be used to determine an average value. Project DC-3 and project DC-5 
have similar result size outputs and only have one waste fraction. It is difficult comparing the results 
per area with different area definitions for each project.  
For the construction year of the building, the same trend is followed as for the hard strip and roof 
demolition. Here project DC-3 (from 1957) and project DC-5 (from 1958) have almost the same impact 
burden. Project DC-1 from 1971 also had an impact, however smaller than for the buildings from the 
50’ties. Project DC-2 is the oldest building from 1895 and has the largest impact on the CC impact 
category.  
The result from the RD-m illustrates the same results (0.01 kg Cu-eq/m2) for all projects, except DC-1, 
which is related to the rounding off. For the normalized values, the trend for decontamination does not 
follow that of the remaining analyzed demolitions as the impact on CCs is up to a factor 10000 greater 
than RD-m. This is the case with RD-m not being impacted by the production of material that is outside 
the scope definition, or the recycling of materials, which cannot be applied to this type of demolition. 
5.6.1 Contribution analysis for the decontamination 
A contribution analysis has been made for the decontamination projects. The contribution from each 
process to the impact categories of CC and RD-m has been illustrated in Figure 34 below, while the 
remaining contribution analysis in each impact category can be seen in the Excel Appendix EASETECH 
results sheet Contribution. Here the demolition process contributes to the largest burden in the CC im-
pact category, except for project DC-1. For RD-m the largest contribution is found for the demolition 
process for projects DC-2, DC-3, and DC-5, while special treatment is the largest contributor to project 
DC-1, and landfilling and transportation contribute the most to project DC-4.

DC-4 only has the contribution from employee commuting, as the demolition period was short enough 
to perform without facilities etc. In most cases, no transport of materials is needed for the decontami-
nation projects as the projects are so small. No trend can therefore be made on the variation of material 
transport and employee commuting. For project DC-1 the transport of equipment and materials is almost 
three times as large as the employee commuting. This project is however more influenced by the special 
treatment process.  
Within the special treatment process for project DC-1, the percentages found in Table 61 are exactly 
similar. The same arguments can thus be used. The incineration process also does not contribute signif-
icantly to any of the decontamination projects. The waste incineration does, however, contribute to a 
small environmental saving for project DC-2 on the RD-m.  
5.6.2 Cost estimate for the decontamination 
The cost of the decontamination activity has been portrayed in Table 59 below. Here the overall cost 
per area for the decontamination projects is similar to that of the soft strip demolition which could be 
related to the small waste amounts. The trend also follows that of the other demolition subcategories 
where the cost of the employees is the largest cost in the demolition activity and overall. For this type 
of demolition, the cost is higher for landfilling and special treatment, as materials are primarily sent 
here. The trend with recycling being the cheapest per ton waste and special treatment the costliest per 
waste is still applied. The transport process is more costly for these demolition types as there is less 
waste to transport, yet still, a transport process is needed.

Project DC-2 is the costliest of the projects per square meter in the decontamination subcategory. This

this type of demolition provides for a better indoor environment for the office buildings of Denmark.

5.7 Contribution analysis for all impact categories 
The contribution analysis for the average values for each demolition subcategory is calculated and vis-
ualized in Table 65. For CC, particulate matter (PM), terrestrial acidification (TA), ionizing radiation 
(IR), POF-eq, and POF-hh are primarily impacted by the demolition process. Within the demolition 
process, the significant impact is related to the burning of fossil carbon dioxide as fuel, whilst for both 
POF, PM, and TA it is the burning of nitrogen oxides from employees commuting. For IR, the transport 
of materials during demolition contributes to emissions of carbon-14 from engine combustion.

Transportation is the process that has the largest impact on freshwater and marine eutrophication (FE 
and ME) as well as ODP. Within each transport process, it is found that the transport contributes to 
emissions of phosphate on the ground, ammonium ion in the surface water, and dinitrogen monoxide 
which contributes to the impact on FE and ME as well as ODP, respectively. Furthermore, landfilling, 
or more specifically the landfilling of incineration ash, has the largest impact on the toxicity processes. 
The impact category of terrestrial ecotoxicity (TE) is, however, primarily controlled by a change in the 
transport or incineration process. This contribution is all caused by chromium, which for construction 
and demolition waste is only a minimal amount as previously discussed. For TE, the substance which 
contributes to the largest impact is copper for both the positive impact from transport and the negative 
impact from incineration. This means as copper is emitted in one process (transport) it is retrieved in 
another (incineration). This is also why this process has an exceedingly small overall impact for the 
normalized values as illustrated in Table 4. 
For CC, material reprocessing and substitution have a larger contribution to the overall impact from 
hard strip demolition. Transport, landfill, and special treatment contribute little to the overall CC impact 
for all the projects. Recycling has the second largest impact on the complete demolition projects.  
For RD-m material reprocessing and substitution contribute to the greatest environmental saving. The 
incineration process also contributes to environmental savings due to the copper retrieval in an electric 
field-enhanced washing during incineration fly ash cleaning (Christensen, 2011). The demolition pro-
cess also contributes to a large positive impact from the electricity consumption. As decontamination 
is the removal of hazardous substances this demolition activity does not include material reprocessing 
and therefore the demolition process has the largest influence related to the electricity consumption. For 
project DC-1 it was found that the process of special treatment alone contributed to the highest (67%) 
positive RD-m impact. During special treatment of waste, considerable amounts of nickel and iron are 
removed from the ground. In all the demolition subcategories, except decontamination, the environ-
mental saving from material reprocessing and substitution is almost outweighed by the resource use 
during demolition, transport, and resource waste during the landfill.

6.0 Sensitivity analysis 
This section presents the sensitivity and uncertainty of the results. This is done by finding the sensitivity 
ratio with a perturbation analysis. Following this, the sensitivity coefficient has been calculated to obtain 
results on the most sensitive parameter to each impact category. Lastly, an uncertainty analysis has been 
made to determine the most uncertain parameters and with this, find the key inputs and parameters to 
the model.  
6.1 Perturbation analysis 
A perturbation analysis has been made to determine which parameters included in each model, were the 
most sensitive to a change in the overall results. Initially, the sensitivity ratio was calculated for each 
parameter in each model, and then the average parameter sensitivity was calculated in each demolition 
subcategory. The sensitivity ratio was calculated with a one-at-a-time analysis where each parameter is 
changed one at a time relative to a variation of 10% to determine the change in the overall result. It was 
decided to normalize the results to get more reliable average results for each demolition subcategory.  
For all the roof demolition projects, the electricity consumption at the demolition site has the greatest 
influence on the change of the results. With a positive value, this indicates that an increase in the pa-
rameter of 10% would also increase the CC impact. The parameter which determines the fraction of 
combustible waste which is incinerated has the second largest influence on the CC impact in the roof 
demolition category. An increase of the parameter by 10% would result in environmental savings and 
thus a decrease in the LCIA impact results. For the façade, the hard strip, and the decontamination 
projects the most sensitive parameter was the Demo_elec.  
For the façade, soft- and hard strip, as well as complete demolition a larger parameter sensitivity has 
been calculated for the recycling of metal which is related to these projects having a larger contribution 
of shredder compared to the other demolition subcategories. The red color for the façade, soft-, and 
hard-strip demolition indicated, that the LCIA results are negative for some projects (RF-1, SS-3, SS-
4, HS-4, and HS-5) despite the positive number result in environmental savings.

Table 67 is opposite that of Table 66, because the LCIA results for the average RD-m impact category 
are negative, indicating a saving. This then illustrates that the blue colors indicate an additional saving, 
while the red colors indicate a burden similar to Table 66.

It was also possible to identify the most sensitive parameters across the impact categories. For all the 
demolition subcategories the fuel utilization at the recycling facility (RGS_diesel) is the most sensitive 
parameter to CC across all impact categories and parameters. Following this is the electricity consump-
tion at the recycling facility (RGS_energy) on CC and the fuel utilization at the recycling facility on the 
Ht-c impact category, except for project CD-2 and HS-4. These parameters all contribute to a burden in 
the sensitive impact category.  
The parameters which are the most sensitive toward a change in the various impact categories also 
depend on the fractions which are recycled. For the roof projects, it varies between the recycling of 
metal (Re_metal) and insulation (Re_insulation) on CC and the recycling of insulation in the terrestrial 
toxicity impact category. For project R-2 with no recycling, the energy transfer coefficient for electricity 
and heat substitution at the incineration plant (ARC_elec and ARC_heat) are the most sensitive in the 
CC and the TET impact category. The same trend is followed for the façade and soft strip demolition, 
and it can thus be seen that for the projects which contain the recycling of metal (R-1 and CD-2) the 
correlated parameter (Re_metal) becomes increasingly sensitive. For some projects, the TET impact 
category is also sensitive to the parameters correlating to the recycling of metal and gypsum (Re_gyp-
sum) and gravel. These are again the projects (RF-2, SS-1, SS-2, and SS-5) with more recyclable 
amounts of gypsum and aggregates. Project SS-6 is similar to project R-2 in sensitivity with only one 
waste fraction of “other combustibles”. The larger decontamination projects which also included waste 
incineration (DC-1 and DC-2) follow this trend for the sensitivity coefficients.  
For the hard strip demolition, the recycling of insulation and gypsum in the terrestrial ecotoxicity impact 
category is the most sensitive together with the recycling of metal in the CC impact category. For the 
complete demolition, which compared to the hard strip demolition, contains a larger amount of heavy 
fraction the parameter related to the recycling of gravel (Re_gravel) and concrete (Re_concrete) be-
comes more sensitive to the TET impact category. For project CD-2 where the heavy fractions are 
crushed and recycled on site the recycling of insulation, gypsum, and metal in the TET, and CC impact 
is more sensitive which is similar to the hard strip demolition.  
 
6.2 Completeness and consistency check 
All known relevant processes for the life cycle inventory have been included in the LCA for each of the 
demolition subcategory projects. Except for the capital goods, no cut-offs were made within the system 
boundary. Capital good was excluded due to lack of data availability; however, the capital goods are 
considered insignificant for the overall results and conclusions as the cut-off is the same for all models 
and across all facilities.  
The system boundaries do not include the material and element production of the waste composition. 
Furthermore, it is assumed that the various waste fractions from the demolition site are without impu-
rities, which could potentially influence the results as the recycling of materials are only possible if the 
==PAGE:118==
101  
 
      LCA on demolition types 
____________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________________ 
waste is sorted accordingly. This has been accounted for in the data quality rating. The same goes for 
the waste treatment fractional division at the recycling facility checked with a DQR for all parameters. 
Numerous other assumptions were made for the modeling such as the transport distances from one 
waste handler to another. The results from this study could, therefore, be subject to change.  
As an iterative process throughout the modeling in this study, a consistency check was performed. This 
was done to ensure that all model projects and analyses of the study were in line with the goal and 
scope.  
The ReCiPe 2016 hierarchic perspective midpoint impact assessment methodology was used as the 
LCIA method to calculate the results of this study. This method has a sufficient degree of impact cov-
erage as found in Table 7. Except for noise and site safety, this impact method covers all expected 
occurring environmental impacts, and thus it is considered both consistent and complete with the goal 
and scope of the study.
